From ʿAlī's sayings we learn that government and power operate under the principle of the welfare of the weak. If God has favoured one of His servants with the gift of knowledge and the consciousness of justice, his responsibility is multiplied. The more God's bounty increases upon a man, the heavier his obligation toward God's creation. In one of his sermons ʿAlī declares: "God's bounty does not increase upon a servant until the right of God grows great in his eyes."

<!-- section 17 (id=3588, raw_sort=17): اللہ کے بارے میں خوش گمانی -->
## A Good Opinion of God (Ḥusn al-Ẓann bi-llāh)

Let us now turn to that portion of Amīr al-Muʾminīn's epistle in which he counsels Mālik al-Ashtar concerning the choice of ministers and advisers. He warns him to keep at a distance from the miserly, the cowardly, and the avaricious, observing that "miserliness, cowardice, and greed all arise from an ill opinion of God."[^cite-40] Such mistrust springs in turn from a failure to grasp the divine disposition (al-fiṭra al-ilāhiyya), and ignorance of that disposition arises from a deficient knowledge of the attributes of God. From this it follows that every human vice can be traced to a corruption of intellect produced by a weakness of faith. Conversely, a firm faith generates a good opinion (ḥusn al-ẓann) of the Most High, a vigorous intellect, and a contemplative apprehension of the divine reality — an apprehension that throws into relief the attributes of God.

One should not infer from the divine character of these attributes that human beings are forbidden to aspire to them. On the contrary, a person must make these qualities his exemplar and his rule of life, polishing them as a remedy for the soul's blemishes. This is not a contention with God's majesty but an effort to become godlike (al-tashabbuh bi-l-Bāriʾ, assimilation to the Originator), and such spiritual ascent is realized only through the worship of God. A person ought to be generous because God is the Generous (al-Karīm), and merciful because God is the Compassionate (al-Raḥmān), the Merciful (al-Raḥīm). And yet, in the very moment of aspiration, he must establish in himself humility and self-effacement, knowing that he can never cultivate within himself any quality truly answerable to the attributes of God.

All the attributes of God testify at once to the complete dependence of the human upon the Divine and to the absolute oneness and self-sufficiency of God. In this way a relation is established between God and His servant that is at once affirmative and apophatic — affirmative in the sense of Mawlānā ʿAlī's saying that whoever does not show mercy to another receives no mercy from God, and apophatic in the sense of God's own declaration:[^cite-41]

> ⟪quran 35:15⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلنَّاسُ أَنتُمُ ٱلْفُقَرَآءُ إِلَى ٱللَّهِۖ وَٱللَّهُ هُوَ ٱلْغَنِىُّ ٱلْحَمِيدُ ‎⟫
> *O mankind! it is you who stand in need of Allah, but Allah is Rich (Free of all wants and needs), Worthy of all praise.*
> O men, it is you who are in need of Allah, and Allah is All-sufficient, All-laudable.
> — **Quran 35:15 (inline widget)**

The synthesis is exact: man's noble character and virtues are reflected images of the divine attributes, while his sober conviction is that, before the majesty of God, he stands in the station of non-being. He is at once the vicegerent of God and His servant — ⟪ar:خلیفۃ اللہ و عبد اللہ⟫. His servanthood holds him within the bounds of service, lest he overstep and presume to participate in the glory of God; his vicegerency reminds him that the awareness of his servitude does not absolve him of the obligation toward God's creation that God Himself has laid upon him. This equilibrium of vicegerency and servanthood — of the divine summons and the human response — is yet another instance of holding all things in their proper place, and the means by which a harmony is forged between moral practice and the purification of the self.

<!-- section 18 (id=3590, raw_sort=18): شہر اور معاشرا -->
## The City and Society

Your letter then turns to the differentiated orders of the polity — armies, scribes, the judiciary, officers, tax-collectors, peasants, merchants, artisans, and the destitute — describing the duties of each. The counsel you give regarding these orders may be characterised as practical justice in action. The ruler is enjoined to discharge the rights of every class, conscious throughout of his duty to God. For it is God who has established these orders, apportioned to each its station and sustenance, and fixed for each its boundaries; and it is He who has taken from the ruler a covenant to abide by the Qurʾan and the sunna.[^cite-42]

The ruler's obligation to society is thus a function of his prior obligation to God, as the verse declares:

> ⟪quran 13:19-20⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ أَفَمَن يَعْلَمُ أَنَّمَآ أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ ٱلْحَقُّ كَمَنْ هُوَ أَعْمَىٰٓ ۚ إِنَّمَا يَتَذَكَّرُ أُو۟لُوا۟ ٱلْأَلْبَبِ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ يُوفُونَ بِعَهْدِ ٱللَّهِ وَلَا يَنقُضُونَ ٱلْمِيثَقَ ‎⟫
> *Shall he then who knows that what has been revealed unto you (O Muhammad PBUH) from your Lord is the truth be like him who is blind? But it is only the men of understanding that pay heed. Those who fulfill the Covenant of Allah and break not the Mîthaq (bond, treaty, covenant);*
> he who knows that what has been revealed to you from your Lord is the truth is like him who is blind? Only those who fulfil the covenant of God and do not break it.
> — **Quran 13:19-20 (inline widget)**

And He has said:

> ⟪quran 17:34⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَا تَقْرَبُوا۟ مَالَ ٱلْيَتِيمِ إِلَّا بِٱلَّتِى هِىَ أَحْسَنُ حَتَّىٰ يَبْلُغَ أَشُدَّهُۥ ۚ وَأَوْفُوا۟ بِٱلْعَهْدِ ۖ إِنَّ ٱلْعَهْدَ كَانَ مَسْـُٔولًۭا ‎⟫
> *And come not near to the orphan's property except to improve it, until he attains the age of full strength. And fulfil (every) covenant. Verily! the covenant, will be questioned about.*
> And do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner, until he reaches maturity. And fulfil your promise; indeed the promise will be called into account.
> — **Quran 17:34 (inline widget)**

You then counsel the master to keep the company of scholars and sages, so that they might bring his government to flourishing and increase upon his land the welfare bequeathed by those who came before. Thereafter you treat each class in turn, and declare the ruler the centre about whom they all revolve. Yet you make plain that the centre is nothing apart from its circumference: it is the circle that joins it to itself, and joins the whole of society to God.

You first take up the army, describing it as a fortress for the people. For the office of commander, you say that the choice must fall upon one who is most sincere toward God, His Messenger, and the Imam of the Time — a man pure of heart, forbearing, slow to anger, swift to forgive, gentle with the weak and firm with the strong. He must not be unmanned by intensity, nor brought to a halt by any weakness. Examined closely, these qualities are conspicuous in the person and character of the Prophet himself (peace be upon him). The emphasis you place upon compassion, gentleness, and forbearance is striking, for these are not the qualities ordinarily cultivated in war, where attachment to combat and to martyrdom prevails. Yet what is required is a courage that is severe only toward the enemy, while remaining tender among one's own, as the Qurʾan declares:

> ⟪quran 48:29⟫
> ⟪ar:‏مُّحَمَّدٌۭ رَّسُولُ ٱللَّهِ ۚ وَٱلَّذِينَ مَعَهُۥٓ أَشِدَّآءُ عَلَى ٱلْكُفَّارِ رُحَمَآءُ بَيْنَهُمْ ۖ تَرَىٰهُمْ رُكَّعًۭا سُجَّدًۭا يَبْتَغُونَ فَضْلًۭا مِّنَ ٱللَّهِ وَرِضْوَنًۭا ۖ سِيمَاهُمْ فِى وُجُوهِهِم مِّنْ أَثَرِ ٱلسُّجُودِ ۚ ذَلِكَ مَثَلُهُمْ فِى ٱلتَّوْرَىٰةِ ۚ وَمَثَلُهُمْ فِى ٱلْإِنجِيلِ كَزَرْعٍ أَخْرَجَ شَطْـَٔهُۥ فَـَٔازَرَهُۥ فَٱسْتَغْلَظَ فَٱسْتَوَىٰ عَلَىٰ سُوقِهِۦ يُعْجِبُ ٱلزُّرَّاعَ لِيَغِيظَ بِهِمُ ٱلْكُفَّارَ ۗ وَعَدَ ٱللَّهُ ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَعَمِلُوا۟ ٱلصَّلِحَتِ مِنْهُم مَّغْفِرَةًۭ وَأَجْرًا عَظِيمًۢا ‎⟫
> *Muhammad (PBUH) is the Messenger of Allah, and those who are with him are severe against disbelievers, and merciful among themselves. You see them bowing and falling down prostrate (in prayer), seeking Bounty from Allah and (His) Good Pleasure. The mark of them (i.e. of their Faith) is on their faces (foreheads) from the traces of (their) prostration (during prayers). This is their description in the Taurat (Torah). But their description in the Injeel (Gospel) is like a (sown) seed which sends forth its shoot, then makes it strong, it then becomes thick, and it stands straight on its stem, delighting the sowers that He may enrage the disbelievers with them. Allah has promised those among them who believe (i.e. all those who follow Islamic Monotheism, the religion of Prophet Muhammad PBUH till the Day of Resurrection) and do righteous good deeds, forgiveness and a mighty reward (i.e. Paradise).*
> Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, and those who are with you are stern against the disbelievers, merciful to one another, so you will see them bowing down and prostrating, seeking Allah's bounty and His pleasure. Then he pulled out a needle, then strengthened it, then it became thick, then she erected on her chins, pleasing the farmers, so that Allah might provoke the disbelievers with them. Allah has promised forgiveness and a great reward to the believers and the doers of good.
> — **Quran 48:29 (inline widget)**

You then describe the qualities required of the judiciary. A judge must not be confused by intricate matters, nor exasperated by the persistence of litigants; he must stand as firm as a mountain in the cause of truth, unsatisfied by surface arguments, sound in disputation, ever seeking what is true, and guarded against the seductions of praise and desire. Such men, you concede, are rare. And you add that their remuneration must be generous, so that they have no need to depend upon the gifts of others.[^cite-43]

With respect to the wise men appointed to office, you say that their integrity must be tested, that no partiality may enter into their selection, and that — even after appointment — their conduct must be examined and any abuse of privilege visited with severe correction.

Regarding the peasants who till the land and from whom the revenue is gathered, you counsel: *Be more concerned with the cultivation of the land than with the revenue, for without cultivation there will be no revenue; whoever imposes a levy without inquiry into the state of cultivation destroys the people.* You add that the ruin of the earth follows from the ruin of those who dwell upon it; and that poverty descends upon them when the souls of the wise are seized by the desire to accumulate, when they grow uncertain of their own continuance, and when they cease to draw benefit from the best of exemplars. To doubt one's continuance, in this idiom, means to imagine oneself immortal and unaccountable. To such, God says:

> ⟪quran 104:1-4⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَيْلٌۭ لِّكُلِّ هُمَزَةٍۢ لُّمَزَةٍ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِى جَمَعَ مَالًۭا وَعَدَّدَهُۥ ‎ ‏يَحْسَبُ أَنَّ مَالَهُۥٓ أَخْلَدَهُۥ ‎ ‏كَلَّا ۖ لَيُنۢبَذَنَّ فِى ٱلْحُطَمَةِ ‎⟫
> *Woe to every slanderer and backbiter. Who has gathered wealth and counted it, He thinks that his wealth will make him last forever! Nay! Verily, he will be thrown into the crushing Fire.*
> Woe to every backbiter and slanderer. He who hoardeth up wealth and counteth it, thinketh that his wealth will abide therein. Nay, but he will surely be thrown into the utter.
> — **Quran 104:1-4 (inline widget)**

When a man forgets that nothing endures, the craving to accumulate takes possession of him: instead of acquiring the true spiritual treasures, he spends his life heaping up the false treasures of matter, and oppression is the inevitable harvest.

On the appointment of officials, you say that the choice must not fall upon those who have only displayed their virtue, but upon those whose past has been examined, whose reputation among the people is known, and whose name is associated with trustworthiness.

Regarding the merchants, you observe that they are of great benefit to society and must be treated with the utmost care. They are by inclination peace-loving and ought to be left to their work; yet the vices that may arise among them — hoarding, miserliness, and deception — must be firmly checked.

### The poor who stand in greatest need

Concerning the poor, the disabled, the orphans, and the elderly, the Commander of the Faithful (Amīr al-Muʾminīn) declares that these are the very people who stand in dire need of the ruler's justice, yet who stretch out their hands to no one. The ruler, he says, must take special care of them if he hopes that God will pardon him on the Day of Resurrection. He must appoint a particular officer charged with attending to their needs and bringing those needs before him. This burden, you concede, is heavy, but God lightens it for those who look toward the Hereafter, who exhort themselves to patience, and who are certain of what God has promised them.[^cite-44]

Here once again you bind the difficulty of the task to spirituality: when a man looks beyond this world toward the next, even the most arduous duty grows light. Those whose horizon does not extend beyond the world will fall into negligence toward these poor. For from a purely worldly standpoint, support of the indigent is politically unprofitable: there is no apparent return to the ruler upon the welfare disbursed in their cause. But when this perspective is joined to a sense of God's all-encompassing justice, His abundant mercy, and the prospect of attaining the Hereafter through the aid given to such people, the burden grows lighter, and the rulers of the age are drawn into sincere service on their behalf.

This disposition transcends ordinary generosity and almsgiving. The man who internalises it will bring the work of relief to a level of perfection; he will not fall prey to self-interest, to egoism, or to negligence — whether deliberate or otherwise. And once this attribute is acquired, the next attribute named by Mawlānā ʿAlī — patience — is born in the same instant; for the act is no longer confined to personal desire or exertion, but is accompanied by divine grace welling up from the roots of faith. Such persons believe in God's reward, His justice, and His promise of mercy, and the ruler's desire to assist the poor and to deal justly with them is strengthened accordingly.

These dispositions, rooted in the reality of the Hereafter, remain hidden from worldly rulers on account of their concealed prejudices and outward corruptions. Such wrongdoers believe neither in the blessings of the Hereafter nor in its torments and chastisements. They are named oppressors because they have disobeyed the requirements of divine justice, and because they have denied that great attribute of mercy which lies at the very essence of the divine nature.

<!-- section 19 (id=3591, raw_sort=19): نیت کی اہمیت -->
## The Importance of Intention

The single most essential element in all the directives you have given is intention. Even where the human task of establishing justice cannot be brought to completion — for this world is itself impermanent — pure intention becomes the foundation of a man's moral life and fills him with the savour of God's grace. If God's promise is true, and if the human being holds firm to that truth, then this conviction works such an effect upon the soul that it transforms a personal endeavour into an unconditional need, and a desirable attribute into an unconditional reality. Morality, in short, is strengthened by spirituality; once this happens, every command of the sharīʿa (Islamic sacred law) becomes light. Thereafter, the human being establishes justice as an act of gratitude for what he has already received from God, and not in expectation of what he is yet to receive in the Hereafter. The Qurʾan speaks of the blessings of Paradise as though they had already been conferred upon the believers, as though what is to come had already passed upon them:

> ⟪quran 108:1-2⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّآ أَعْطَيْنَكَ ٱلْكَوْثَرَ ‎ ‏فَصَلِّ لِرَبِّكَ وَٱنْحَرْ ‎⟫
> *Verily, We have granted you (O Muhammad (Peace be upon him)) Al-Kauthar (a river in Paradise); Therefore turn in prayer to your Lord and sacrifice (to Him only).*
> indeed We have given you abundance, so perform the prayer to your Lord and sacrifice.
> — **Quran 108:1-2 (inline widget)**

Mawlānā ʿAlī, expounding this point, has said:

There is a group that worships God out of desire for Paradise — and this is the worship of merchants; another that worships out of fear of Hell — and this is the worship of slaves; and a third group that worships God out of gratitude — and this is the worship of the free.[^cite-45] For this reason he said in another place: ⟪ar-quote:یا الہی ما عبدتک من شوق جنتک ولا من خوف النارک بل وجدت مستحق لی العبادۃ فالعبدتک⟫ — *O God, I have not worshipped You out of longing for Your Paradise, nor out of fear of Your Fire; rather, I have found You worthy of worship, and so I have worshipped You.*

The human being is acquitted of the deficiencies of his deeds through the sincerity of his intention; his act is measured not by its outcome but by the purity of its motive. The matter is not to establish justice for some worldly purpose, but to establish justice in order to satisfy the requirements of justice itself — for the justice of the Just One is intrinsic to His very purpose. Justice is not attributed to God in the sense that He merely performs just acts; rather, He is the Just, and on that account His acts are just. For this reason, the just person acts justly in order to align with the will of God — not merely to obey His command, and certainly not from any worldly avidity. God has said:

> ⟪quran 92:17-21⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَسَيُجَنَّبُهَا ٱلْأَتْقَى ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِى يُؤْتِى مَالَهُۥ يَتَزَكَّىٰ ‎ ‏وَمَا لِأَحَدٍ عِندَهُۥ مِن نِّعْمَةٍۢ تُجْزَىٰٓ ‎ ‏إِلَّا ٱبْتِغَآءَ وَجْهِ رَبِّهِ ٱلْأَعْلَىٰ ‎ ‏وَلَسَوْفَ يَرْضَىٰ ‎⟫
> *And Al-Muttaqun (the pious and righteous - see V.2:2) will be far removed from it (Hell). He who spends his wealth for increase in self-purification, And have in his mind no favour from anyone for which a reward is expected in return, Except only the desire to seek the Countenance of his Lord, the Most High; He surely will be pleased (when he will enter Paradise).*
>And away from it is the God-fearing one who gives his wealth so that he may be purified; and no soul has any favour against it, but he who is recompensed only with the Countenance of his Lord, the Most High, and he shall soon be well-pleased.
> — **Quran 92:17-21 (inline widget)**

And this is the very definition of ⟪ar:’ابرار‘⟫ — the righteous. The scholars are unanimous that these verses were revealed in honour of Mawlānā ʿAlī and his family on the occasion when, having fasted three days, they gave away the bread of their own household to a poor man, an orphan, and a captive:[^cite-46]

> ⟪quran 76:8-9⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَيُطْعِمُونَ ٱلطَّعَامَ عَلَىٰ حُبِّهِۦ مِسْكِينًۭا وَيَتِيمًۭا وَأَسِيرًا ‎ ‏إِنَّمَا نُطْعِمُكُمْ لِوَجْهِ ٱللَّهِ لَا نُرِيدُ مِنكُمْ جَزَآءًۭ وَلَا شُكُورًا ‎⟫
> *And they give food, inspite of their love for it (or for the love of Him), to Miskin (poor), the orphan, and the captive, (Saying): "We feed you seeking Allah's Countenance only. We wish for no reward, nor thanks from you.*
> And they feed the needy, and the orphan, and the captive, out of love for Him. Whatever We feed you, it is only for the sake of Allah; it is not for us to take any retribution from you, nor to be grateful.
> — **Quran 76:8-9 (inline widget)**

The more an action is performed for God's sake — neither bent by praise nor turned aside by blame, neither weakened by outward difficulty nor inflated by inward consequence — the more it is acceptable. This relation between intention and act is what allows us to grasp ʿAlī's saying that his heart was in Paradise even as his body laboured in the world. Worldly morality is strong or weak according to outcome: if a good result is obtained, the agent persists; if not, he desists. But the act of one whose gaze is fixed upon the Hereafter is completed by the very sincerity of its intention. This is not to say that pure intention dispenses one from prolonged effort; on the contrary, the proof of sincere intention is that, whatever the outcome, the agent continues in the doing of good and never wearies or relents. An action that ceases under pressure of circumstance, or that shrinks to a merely formal performance, betrays a defect of intention. Pure intention requires that one strive without flagging and without relent in pursuit of one's purpose.

The just man never despairs, and never abandons the struggle to establish justice. His aim is not to establish justice in order to attain any particular outcome; rather, his intention is the spiritual fulfilment of his own justice, and he recognises that his bodily existence is bound to external circumstance and, ultimately, to the pleasure of God. Yet such a one does not grow weary at the absence of results: his exertion is as enduring as his certainty of the permanence of God's blessing. His acts are reinforced by the very attribute of divine justice.

Jesus Christ has said: *When the principles of justice are considered apart from spirituality, what arises is a personal idealism — and being shaped by external consequences, it is broken under the pressure of difficulty and despair.* But when spirituality is joined to justice, the indestructible attribute of divine justice underwrites it and bestows upon it the power of completion. To the degree that a person's principles are grounded in spiritual knowledge, despair and discouragement cannot touch him in the face of hardship. We find the example of this in the very biography of the Commander of the Faithful: when he came to the apparent caliphate, storms of war and rebellion arose one upon another, so that he could not bring to fruition all the principles and strategies he had wished to implement; yet through all these difficulties he was never disappointed, never dejected, never depressed, nor was he ever wanting in effort or in the pursuit of justice. He used to say that no action is ordinary if piety (taqwā) is joined to it — meaning that despair is alien to the pious man, for he is ever conscious of the truth. This degree of piety becomes for him a way out of the injustices of this world, as ʿAlī (peace be upon him) said: ⟪ar-quote:من یتق اللہ یجعل لہ مخرجا من الفتن وا نور من الظلم⟫ — *Whoever fears God, God will make for him a way out from trials and a light amid darkness.*[^cite-47]

<!-- section 20 (id=3592, raw_sort=20): عبادت کی فضیلت -->
## The Excellence of Worship

We turn now to the portion of your letter in which you describe the essence of all virtues, namely prayer. At the outset of the epistle, you counsel the ruler to attend to the duties of worship; here you instruct the governor to devote the choicest part of his time to working for the sake of God, once his administrative tasks have been discharged—and to make of that time an act of worship and consecration. Mālik al-Ashtar is specifically enjoined to purify his religion through the obligatory acts and to refine his devotion.[^cite-48] The aim of this purification is to clarify the very substance of his relation to God through worship—a relation that subsists between the soul and its Lord—because the depth of that relation is the hallmark of every human excellence.

"Know," he says, "that all your works depend upon your worship." By worship here is meant not merely the outward bowing and prostration, but the inner sincerity that animates and sustains the outward act. It is the sincerity, intensity, and completeness of prayer that bring it to its purpose. "Expend the strength of your nights and days for the sake of God; complete the deeds by which you draw near to Him, and perform them without weariness, taking your body to its utmost." The decisive term here is *qurb*, nearness—that which one obtains from the devotion arising in the depths of the heart amid the solitude of the darkest watch of night. All outward action is governed by the purity of intention with which a person surrenders himself to God. If the inner life is suffused with the light of prayer, its rays will inevitably break forth in outward conduct. For this reason Mālik is bidden to dedicate the best portion of his time to God: every part of the ruler's time is, in truth, for God, provided the intention behind action is sound and provided that intention safeguards the people. At such a moment the wellsprings of obligatory and supererogatory prayer flow over into the outward life. The Prophet's ḥadīth conveys this beautiful truth:

> I love nothing by which My servant draws near to Me more than what I have made obligatory upon him. My servant continues to draw near to Me by supererogatory acts (nawāfil) until I love him. Then I become the hearing by which he hears, the sight by which he sees, the hand by which he grasps, and the foot by which he walks.

To see and to act through God is to perceive things according to their reality and then to act in accordance with that perception. In a second sense, it is to render justice: to place each thing in its proper station. Note in this ḥadīth that obligatory and supererogatory prayers are alike the cause of nearness to God, but the locus of divine action through the servant is the *nawāfil*, not the obligatory prayers. As ʿAlī says, take your actions to the utmost of your body. By this he showed that there is no benefit in mere careless or mechanical worship. "The example of one who prays without striving (ijtihād)," he said, "is that of an archer who looses his arrow without a bow."

As noted earlier, to draw near to God and to attain oneness with Him (tawḥīd) requires the integral purification of the self, and prayer is the very arena in which the deepest dimensions of the soul are disclosed, cultivated, and gathered into unity. The fulfilment of prayer therefore lies not in tongue and limb alone, but in the whole of soul and body. God says:

> ⟪quran 24:36⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فِى بُيُوتٍ أَذِنَ ٱللَّهُ أَن تُرْفَعَ وَيُذْكَرَ فِيهَا ٱسْمُهُۥ يُسَبِّحُ لَهُۥ فِيهَا بِٱلْغُدُوِّ وَٱلْآصَالِ ‎⟫
> *In houses (mosques), which Allah has ordered to be raised (to be cleaned, and to be honoured), in them His Name is glorified in the mornings and in the afternoons or the evenings,*
> in houses which Allah has commanded to be venerated and His name to be remembered therein, glorifying Him in the mornings and evenings.
> — **Quran 24:36 (inline widget)**

Concerning nearness to God, ʿAlī (peace be upon him) said: "Indeed, God has set within hearts a remembrance by which they hear though they were deaf, see though they were blind, and become tractable though they were defiant."[^cite-49] In every age there have been singular servants of God with whom He converses in their inmost thoughts and whom He addresses through the essence of their intellect. They diffuse His light through their hearts, hearings, and sights, and summon the people to the remembrance of the Days of God.

The importance of *dhikr* (remembrance) is evident in your words: through it the consciousness of truth is engendered. By *dhikr* you mean not only the act of invoking a particular name of God, but the constant awareness of God that constitutes the essence of all worship, contemplation, and reflection. Its aim is not merely to call God to mind, or to repeat His names, but to contemplate His divine realities through the outward and inward manifestations of those names. Elsewhere, our master ʿAlī says of *dhikr*:

Indeed, there are men who have so taken up the remembrance of God that neither commerce nor livelihood diverts them from it; they pass the days of their lives within it. He exhorts the people to justice and himself stands as its embodiment—that is, such men themselves show how each thing is to be placed in its proper station and explain by their conduct how that placement is to be sustained. Spirituality is not a thing separate from action; it is a connection to the moral depths from which the very relation to the ⟪ar:’الہ‘⟫ proceeds, a relation realised through worship in general and through remembrance in particular. Just as sincerity of intention has been shown to be the fundamental substance, so it now becomes clear that sincerity is the cardinal element of prayer—the element that takes the body to its utmost. If the attribute of justice belongs to the very essence of God, then the closer a person draws to Him through prayer and remembrance, the more just he becomes, cultivating in himself positive capacities, spirituality, intellect, and ethical excellence. Justice is at once the cause of worship and its consequence. The bond between worship and justice thereby becomes plain. Justice teaches us to place each thing in its proper station. Justice toward God is therefore fulfilled through prayer and remembrance, while justice toward oneself and toward others is the fruit of the nearness obtained through the worship of God—from which every virtue flows.

The Commander of the Faithful has emphasised both outward prayer and its inward source, *dhikr*. Plato held that the highest knowledge is knowledge of the ⟪ar:’خیر‘⟫ (the Good), because from it every good and every rightness is born. If a person were to acquire all the sciences of the world but did not acquire the science of the Good, he would have acquired nothing. He drew a comparison between the Good and the sun, saying that what the sun does for sight, the Good does for intellect and being.

Once Dhiʿlab al-Yamānī asked ʿAlī, "Have you seen Him whom you worship?" He answered, "Had I not seen Him, I would not have worshipped Him." Asked, "How did you see Him?" he replied, "Eyes do not apprehend Him by the sight of vision, but hearts apprehend Him by the realities of faith."[^cite-50] From this saying we learn the surpassing importance of *dhikr*, for it is by *dhikr* that the heart becomes pure and clear. Yet *dhikr* does not refer to the knowledge of outward forms; it is rather the knowledge of the Fashioner of those forms, described in Islam as the divine attribute ⟪ar:’المصور‘⟫ (the Form-Giver). But this remembrance cannot be expressed in words as other sciences can. It demands tireless worship and unwearying labour by night and day. It is like the spark struck from the friction of long effort: once the candle is lit, it never again goes out, but continues to feed itself throughout one's life.

The substance of justice in the Commander of the Faithful's letter is this: for any society to establish justice, it must grasp these Islamic concepts not only by the intellect but by the heart, for the heart is what is joined to the inner realities and only the heart can perceive them by the light of God. Nor is this possible without His grace. The prophetic requirement of justice is that all the components of human life—worship, ethics, action—be brought into harmony with one another upon the principle of *tawḥīd*. The world of ethics cannot be sundered from the world of spirituality, because in ʿAlī's view true virtue is that which is original, strengthened, and turned toward the truth. These requirements of justice within the soul can be brought about only through worship whose spirit is *dhikr*. This final analysis alone establishes that *dhikr* is the very standard by which human excellence is measured.

<!-- section 21 (id=3602, raw_sort=21): ذکر اللہ -->
## The Remembrance of God (Dhikr Allāh)

ʿAlī al-Hujwīrī, author of *Kashf al-Maḥjūb*, writes of Mawlānā ʿAlī (peace be upon him) as one who was drowned in the tempestuous sea of trials and engulfed by the flames of love — the chief of the purified and the friends of God (*muqtadā al-awliyāʾ wa-l-aṣfiyāʾ*). His station upon this path is exalted and noble. So complete was his command of the realities of the higher world that Junayd is reported to have said, "ʿAlī is our shaykh in the principles and in the trials" (*shaykhunā fī al-uṣūl wa-l-balāyā*). He is the imām of every intellectual and practical domain. The Sufis interpret the knowledge of this path as a matter of principle, and they reckon its practice as the patient endurance of calamities.

This forbearance amid calamities recalls the saying of the Commander of the Faithful (Amīr al-Muʾminīn) that he loved those from whom the ignorant felt estranged. What the Sufis loved — and the ignorant shunned — was worship, and within worship the singular attentiveness to the Creator that excludes every other claim. Their worship is thus a kind of dying: a sacrifice of every other occupation upon the path of attentiveness to God. They efface themselves for the sake of the Real.

The word *dhikr* is remarkably full in its meaning and has no exact substitute in other languages. It bears the senses of remembrance, reflection, complaint, repetition, glorification, and recitation. It also carries the meanings of mention, statement, praise, celebration, fame, eminence, honour, admonition, and warning. God has likewise named the Qurʾān itself *dhikr*.

The most precise definition is offered by al-Rāghib al-Iṣfahānī, who writes in his lexicon that *dhikr* is the faculty by which a person preserves cognition and knowledge. While *ḥifẓ* (memory) is taken in the sense of holding the past, *dhikr* is taken in the sense of bringing forth — making present. *Dhikr* is also described as the presence of a thing in a person's consciousness and on his tongue. Hence the Sufi distinction between two kinds of *dhikr* — the remembrance of the heart and the remembrance of the tongue — each of which further divides in two: the *dhikr* that is intermittent, broken by lapses, and the *dhikr* that is unbroken and continual. True remembrance of God is possible only through the presence of the heart.

The remembrance of God names a consciousness that absorbs the divine being, but it is at the same time a process unfolding under spiritual discipline. *Dhikr* is therefore both end and means, knowledge and the action that approaches knowledge. For the Sufis and the friends of God it constitutes a system through which the heart absorbs what is held in the intellect, transforming an abstract object of thought into the concrete presence of a spiritual reality.[^cite-51] If prayer is the foundation of worship, the remembrance of God stands higher still, as the Qurʾān declares:

> ⟪quran 29:45⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱتْلُ مَآ أُوحِىَ إِلَيْكَ مِنَ ٱلْكِتَبِ وَأَقِمِ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ ۖ إِنَّ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ تَنْهَىٰ عَنِ ٱلْفَحْشَآءِ وَٱلْمُنكَرِ ۗ وَلَذِكْرُ ٱللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ مَا تَصْنَعُونَ ‎⟫
> *Recite (O Muhammad PBUH) what has been revealed to you of the Book (the Quran), and perform As-Salat (Iqamat&shy;as&shy;Salat). Verily, As-Salat (the prayer) prevents from Al-Fahsha' (i.e. great sins of every kind, unlawful sexual intercourse, etc.) and Al-Munkar (i.e. disbelief, polytheism, and every kind of evil wicked deed, etc.) and the remembering (praising, etc.) of (you by) Allah (in front of the angels) is greater indeed [than your remembering (praising, etc.) Allah in prayers, etc.]. And Allah knows what you do.*
> Recite what has been revealed to you of the Book, and be steadfast in prayer. Indeed, prayer prevents indecency and evil, and the remembrance of God is greater; and God is aware of what you do.
> — **Quran 29:45 (inline widget)**

Numerous prophetic traditions extol the virtue of remembrance. The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) once said to his Companions: "Shall I not tell you of the purest of your deeds, the one that holds the highest rank among you, that exceeds the giving of gold and silver, and the meeting of your enemies in battle?" When they urged him to tell, he replied, "It is your unceasing remembrance of God." In a similar vein Mawlānā ʿAlī says, "Remember God continually, for He illuminates the hearts; it is the most excellent of worship." Imām Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq (peace be upon him) said, "Our Shīʿa are those who, when they are alone, mention God much."

---

### The Remembrance of God Polishes the Hearts

Let us now return to the sermon of the Commander of the Faithful in which he said that the remembrance of God is a burnishing for the hearts. He opened by reciting:

> ⟪quran 24:35-37⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ ٱللَّهُ نُورُ ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ ۚ مَثَلُ نُورِهِۦ كَمِشْكَوٰةٍۢ فِيهَا مِصْبَاحٌ ۖ ٱلْمِصْبَاحُ فِى زُجَاجَةٍ ۖ ٱلزُّجَاجَةُ كَأَنَّهَا كَوْكَبٌۭ دُرِّىٌّۭ يُوقَدُ مِن شَجَرَةٍۢ مُّبَرَكَةٍۢ زَيْتُونَةٍۢ لَّا شَرْقِيَّةٍۢ وَلَا غَرْبِيَّةٍۢ يَكَادُ زَيْتُهَا يُضِىٓءُ وَلَوْ لَمْ تَمْسَسْهُ نَارٌۭ ۚ نُّورٌ عَلَىٰ نُورٍۢ ۗ يَهْدِى ٱللَّهُ لِنُورِهِۦ مَن يَشَآءُ ۚ وَيَضْرِبُ ٱللَّهُ ٱلْأَمْثَلَ لِلنَّاسِ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَىْءٍ عَلِيمٌۭ ‎ ‏فِى بُيُوتٍ أَذِنَ ٱللَّهُ أَن تُرْفَعَ وَيُذْكَرَ فِيهَا ٱسْمُهُۥ يُسَبِّحُ لَهُۥ فِيهَا بِٱلْغُدُوِّ وَٱلْآصَالِ ‎ ‏رِجَالٌۭ لَّا تُلْهِيهِمْ تِجَرَةٌۭ وَلَا بَيْعٌ عَن ذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ وَإِقَامِ ٱلصَّلَوٰةِ وَإِيتَآءِ ٱلزَّكَوٰةِ ۙ يَخَافُونَ يَوْمًۭا تَتَقَلَّبُ فِيهِ ٱلْقُلُوبُ وَٱلْأَبْصَرُ ‎⟫
> *Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth. The parable of His Light is as (if there were) a niche and within it a lamp, the lamp is in glass, the glass as it were a brilliant star, lit from a blessed tree, an olive, neither of the east (i.e. neither it gets sun-rays only in the morning) nor of the west (i.e. nor it gets sun-rays only in the afternoon,  but it is exposed to the sun all day long), whose oil would almost glow forth (of itself), though no fire touched it. Light upon Light! Allah guides to His Light whom He wills. And Allah sets forth parables for mankind, and Allah is All-Knower of everything. In houses (mosques), which Allah has ordered to be raised (to be cleaned, and to be honoured), in them His Name is glorified in the mornings and in the afternoons or the evenings, Men whom neither trade nor sale diverts them from the Remembrance of Allah (with heart and tongue), nor from performing As&shy;Salat (Iqamat-as-Salat),  nor from giving the Zakat. They fear a Day when hearts and eyes will be overturned (from the horror of the torment of the Day of Resurrection).*
> God is the light of the heavens and the earth. The likeness of His light is as a niche wherein is a lamp; the lamp in a glass, the glass as it were a glittering star kindled from a blessed olive tree, neither of the east nor of the west, whose oil would almost shine forth though no fire touched it: Light upon Light. God guides to His light whom He wills, and He sets forth parables for mankind; God is the Knower of all things. In houses that God has permitted to be raised, and wherein His name is remembered: in them He is glorified, at morning and at evening, by men whom neither commerce nor trade distracts from the remembrance of God, nor from the performance of prayer, nor from the giving of alms; they fear a Day on which hearts and eyes will be overturned.
> — **Quran 24:35-37 (inline widget)**

When he reached the verse concerning the men whom neither trade nor commerce diverts from the remembrance of God, he said:

Indeed, God has appointed the remembrance of God a polish for the hearts, by which they hear after deafness, see after blindness, and submit after resistance. In every age there have been servants of God to whom He has spoken through the inmost stirrings of thought and intellect. He has spread the light of guidance through their hearing, their sight, and the light kindled in their hearts, and through them He has summoned the people to the days of remembrance. Among them is a particular cohort who prefer the remembrance of God in this world to every other remembrance, so that no trade or commerce distracts them from it. They spend the days of their lives in this *dhikr*. They live in this world as though they had already crossed into the Hereafter — at once present here and dwelling there.

The link between this burnishing of hearts and the Verse of Light is evident. The hidden light of the heart is drawn forth by the remembrance of God. The inward light of consciousness is set in relief⟪ar:نُور ⟪ar:علی نُور⟫ by the outward light of *dhikr*. When the heart is illumined, its effect spreads to every limb, and they too are illumined — which is then how ⟪ar:نُور⟫ illuminates the meaning of the heart ⟪ar:علی نُور⟫. To "polish" here means to cleanse, to unveil, to bring into the light. As God says:

> ⟪quran 92:1-2⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱلَّيْلِ إِذَا يَغْشَىٰ ‎ ‏وَٱلنَّهَارِ إِذَا تَجَلَّىٰ ‎⟫
> *By the night as it envelops; And by the day as it appears in brightness;*
> By the night when it envelops, and by the day when it shines forth.
> — **Quran 92:1-2 (inline widget)**

Here God swears by the night when it envelops — by what was not, and now has appeared — and by the day when it shines forth, what was not, and is now manifest. Elsewhere He says:

> ⟪quran 91:3⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱلنَّهَارِ إِذَا جَلَّىٰهَا ‎⟫
> *And by the day as it shows up (the sun's) brightness;*
> And by the day when it reveals it.
> — **Quran 91:3 (inline widget)**

That is, through the remembrance of God the consciousness latent within the heart is awakened and made manifest in action. The role of the heart here deserves close attention. On the one hand the heart receives the divine light through *dhikr* and reflects it upon every limb; on the other hand *dhikr* awakens within its depths a dormant consciousness that discloses the truths to it. Mawlānā ʿAlī named this hidden consciousness ⟪ar:’دفائن العقول‘⟫ — *the buried treasures of intellects*. It thus becomes clear that the true purpose of *dhikr* is the purification of the heart, the commanding centre (*amīr al-badan*) and the seat of authentic insight.[^cite-52]

God says:

> ⟪quran 7:179⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًۭا مِّنَ ٱلْجِنِّ وَٱلْإِنسِ ۖ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌۭ لَّا يَفْقَهُونَ بِهَا وَلَهُمْ أَعْيُنٌۭ لَّا يُبْصِرُونَ بِهَا وَلَهُمْ آذَانٌۭ لَّا يَسْمَعُونَ بِهَآ ۚ أُو۟لَٓئِكَ كَٱلْأَنْعَمِ بَلْ هُمْ أَضَلُّ ۚ أُو۟لَٓئِكَ هُمُ ٱلْغَفِلُونَ ‎⟫
> *And surely, We have created many of the jinns and mankind for Hell.&nbsp;They have hearts wherewith they understand not, they have eyes wherewith they see not, and they have ears wherewith they hear not (the truth). They are like cattle, nay even more astray; those! They are the heedless ones.*
> And We have prepared for Hell many of the jinn and mankind. They have hearts with which they do not understand, eyes with which they do not see, and ears with which they do not hear. They are like cattle — nay, further astray. It is they who are the heedless.
> — **Quran 7:179 (inline widget)**

Here God uses the word *al-ghāfilūn* — the heedless. Heedlessness (*ghafla*) is the very antithesis of *dhikr*, which is to be present and to remember. The verse that follows describes the mystical practice of *dhikr*:

> ⟪quran 7:180⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلِلَّهِ ٱلْأَسْمَآءُ ٱلْحُسْنَىٰ فَٱدْعُوهُ بِهَا ۖ وَذَرُوا۟ ٱلَّذِينَ يُلْحِدُونَ فِىٓ أَسْمَٓئِهِۦ ۚ سَيُجْزَوْنَ مَا كَانُوا۟ يَعْمَلُونَ ‎⟫
> *And (all) the Most Beautiful Names belong to Allah, so call on Him by them, and leave the company of those who belie or deny (or utter impious speech against) His Names. They will be requited for what they used to do.*
> To God belong the Most Beautiful Names; so call upon Him by them, and leave those who blaspheme His names — they will be recompensed for what they used to do.
> — **Quran 7:180 (inline widget)**

In the verses that follow, the bond between remembrance and the heart becomes still clearer:

> ⟪quran 13:28⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَتَطْمَىِٕنُّ قُلُوبُهُم بِذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ ۗ أَلَا بِذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ تَطْمَىِٕنُّ ٱلْقُلُوبُ ‎⟫
> *Those who believe (in the Oneness of Allah - Islamic Monotheism), and whose hearts find rest in the remembrance of Allah, Verily, in the remembrance of Allah do hearts find rest.*
> Those who believe, and whose hearts are at peace in the remembrance of God — surely in the remembrance of God do hearts find peace.
> — **Quran 13:28 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 50:37⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّ فِى ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَىٰ لِمَن كَانَ لَهُۥ قَلْبٌ أَوْ أَلْقَى ٱلسَّمْعَ وَهُوَ شَهِيدٌۭ ‎⟫
> *Verily, therein is indeed a reminder for him who has a heart or gives ear while he is heedful.*
> Surely in that is a reminder for one who has a heart, or who lends an ear while he is a witness.
> — **Quran 50:37 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 8:2⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّمَا ٱلْمُؤْمِنُونَ ٱلَّذِينَ إِذَا ذُكِرَ ٱللَّهُ وَجِلَتْ قُلُوبُهُمْ وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ آيَتُهُۥ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَنًۭا وَعَلَىٰ رَبِّهِمْ يَتَوَكَّلُونَ ‎⟫
> *The believers are only those who, when Allah is mentioned, feel a fear in their hearts and when His Verses (this Quran) are recited unto them, they (i.e. the Verses) increase their Faith; and they put their trust in their Lord (Alone);*
> The believers are only those whose hearts tremble when God is mentioned; when His verses are recited to them, their faith is increased, and upon their Lord they rely.
> — **Quran 8:2 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 39:22⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَفَمَن شَرَحَ ٱللَّهُ صَدْرَهُۥ لِلْإِسْلَمِ فَهُوَ عَلَىٰ نُورٍۢ مِّن رَّبِّهِۦ ۚ فَوَيْلٌۭ لِّلْقَسِيَةِ قُلُوبُهُم مِّن ذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ ۚ أُو۟لَٓئِكَ فِى ضَلَلٍۢ مُّبِينٍ ‎⟫
> *Is he whose breast Allah has opened to Islam, so that he is in light from His Lord (as he who is non-Muslim)? So, woe to those whose hearts are hardened against remembrance of Allah! They are in plain error!*
> Is one whose breast God has opened to Islam, so that he stands in a light from his Lord — woe, then, to those whose hearts are hardened against the remembrance of God; they are in manifest error.
> — **Quran 39:22 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 39:23⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱللَّهُ نَزَّلَ أَحْسَنَ ٱلْحَدِيثِ كِتَبًۭا مُّتَشَبِهًۭا مَّثَانِىَ تَقْشَعِرُّ مِنْهُ جُلُودُ ٱلَّذِينَ يَخْشَوْنَ رَبَّهُمْ ثُمَّ تَلِينُ جُلُودُهُمْ وَقُلُوبُهُمْ إِلَىٰ ذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ ۚ ذَلِكَ هُدَى ٱللَّهِ يَهْدِى بِهِۦ مَن يَشَآءُ ۚ وَمَن يُضْلِلِ ٱللَّهُ فَمَا لَهُۥ مِنْ هَادٍ ‎⟫
> *Allah has sent down the best statement, a Book (this Quran), its parts resembling each other in goodness and truth, oft-repeated.&nbsp;The skins of those who fear their Lord shiver from it (when they recite it or hear it). Then their skin and their heart soften to the remembrance of Allah. That is the guidance of Allah. He Guides therewith whom He pleases and whomever Allah sends astray, for him there is no guide.*
> God has sent down the most beautiful discourse — a Book consistent within itself, paired in repetition — from which the skins of those who fear their Lord shiver; then their skins and hearts soften to the remembrance of God. That is God's guidance, by which He guides whom He wills; and whomever God leads astray has no guide.
> — **Quran 39:23 (inline widget)**

These verses make clear that *dhikr* is intimately bound to the heart, which is what renders consciousness real. The heart must be enriched by remembrance. The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said, "For everything there is a polish, and the polish of the heart is the remembrance of God." This hadith points to a disciplined practice in which consciousness and action are joined, returning us to that other saying of the Prophet: "Worship God as though you see Him." On the one side, *dhikr* may take the form of purification of thought and reflection; on the other, of regular action — its various forms being ⟪ar:تسبیح⟫ (*tasbīḥ*, glorification), ⟪ar:تمجید⟫ (*tamjīd*, magnification), ⟪ar:تحمید⟫ (*taḥmīd*, praise), ⟪ar:تقدیس⟫ (*taqdīs*, sanctification), and ⟪ar:تحلیل⟫ (*tahlīl*, the declaration of divine unity) — each of which removes the rust of forgetfulness from the heart. The Messenger of God described such practitioners as the people of remembrance (*ahl al-dhikr*), saying: "God has angels who roam the roads in search of the people of remembrance; when they find them, they call to one another, 'Come to that which you have been seeking.'"

Consider now the ⟪ar:بصیرت⟫ — the *baṣīra*, the inner sight — by which the heart sees through the remembrance of God: it is purified by *dhikr* and revived by faith. Mawlānā ʿAlī says this insight comes by inspiration and is no less than the very sight of God Himself. Elsewhere he calls the pure heart the locus of beholding God: "Whoever purifies his heart, sees God." The bond between purity of heart and remembrance is plain in his words: "Where are those whose deeds are sincerely for the sake of God, who purify their hearts with His remembrance until the heart becomes a resting-place for Him?" This is aligned with the well-known prophetic hadith: "My servant does not cease drawing nearer to Me through supererogatory devotions until I love him; and when I love him, I become his hearing by which he hears, his sight by which he sees, his hand by which he grasps, and his foot by which he walks." Human perception and action are thus drawn into the field of divine reality, and the heart gains spiritual insight. At this stage the heart — the seat of consciousness — transcends the order of physicality; its inmost essence (its *sirr*) is joined to the truth, and that essence begins to see as God sees, finally beholding God Himself.

Abū Ṭālib al-Makkī (a Sufi) writes that if it is true that God enters into human perception through human action, then the highest reach of spiritual consciousness must be that sight of God is no longer the seeing of God *by* the servant but becomes the seeing of God *through* God, by way of the heart. Mawlānā ʿAlī formulates this philosophy in his maxim: ⟪ar-quote:أعرف اللہ با اللہ⟫ — "Know God through God." In this final reckoning, God is at once the seer and the seen, as He says:

> ⟪quran 85:3⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَشَاهِدٍۢ وَمَشْهُودٍۢ ‎⟫
> *And by the witnessing day (i.e. Friday), and by the witnessed day [i.e. the day of 'Arafat (Hajj) the ninth of Dhul-Hîjjah];*
> And by the witness and the witnessed.
> — **Quran 85:3 (inline widget)**

What witnesses is the inmost essence of human consciousness; what is witnessed is divine reality. A person's deep faith renders God present in every place and at every moment, as Mawlānā ʿAlī said in his sermon: "By Your majesty that fills all things, by Your names that are the foundation of every thing, by Your knowledge that encompasses every thing, by the light of Your face through which every thing has been illumined."

Nothing can subsist without its ground. The name of God — or the conjunction of His names — supplies that ground. To see things rightly is to see the divine attributes, which alone are real, and the things themselves as the physical reflection of those names, their manifestations and self-disclosures. When the heart is purified by *dhikr*, it finds God everywhere. The presence of God in all things is mentioned in the Qurʾān from two angles — one from the side of God, the other from the side of the human being:

> ⟪quran 2:115⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلِلَّهِ ٱلْمَشْرِقُ وَٱلْمَغْرِبُ ۚ فَأَيْنَمَا تُوَلُّوا۟ فَثَمَّ وَجْهُ ٱللَّهِ ۚ إِنَّ ٱللَّهَ وَسِعٌ عَلِيمٌۭ ‎⟫
> *And to Allah belong the east and the west, so wherever you turn yourselves or your faces there is the Face of Allah (and He is High above, over His Throne). Surely! Allah is All-Sufficient for His creatures' needs, All-Knowing.*
> To God belong the East and the West; wherever you turn, there is the Face of God. Truly God is All-Embracing, All-Knowing.
> — **Quran 2:115 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 41:53⟫
> ⟪ar:‏سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَتِنَا فِى ٱلأٓفَاقِ وَفِىٓ أَنفُسِهِمْ حَتَّىٰ يَتَبَيَّنَ لَهُمْ أَنَّهُ ٱلْحَقُّ ۗ أَوَلَمْ يَكْفِ بِرَبِّكَ أَنَّهُۥ عَلَىٰ كُلِّ شَىْءٍۢ شَهِيدٌ ‎⟫
> *&nbsp;We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their ownselves, until it becomes manifest to them that this (the Quran) is the truth. Is it not sufficient in regard to your Lord that He is a Witness over all things?*
> We shall show them Our signs on the horizons and within themselves until it becomes clear to them that it is the truth. Is it not enough that your Lord is Witness over all things?
> — **Quran 41:53 (inline widget)**

The heart purified by *dhikr* can read these signs everywhere and in every thing — but such vision does not reduce God to the human sensible. Mawlānā ʿAlī said, "God is in everything, yet not by way of proximity; and He is apart from every thing, yet not by way of separation." God's accompanying of things is not a nearness of contiguity, nor a diminution of the divine. He is infinitely nearer to us than we are able to be near to Him, as He says:

> ⟪quran 50:16⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا ٱلْإِنسَنَ وَنَعْلَمُ مَا تُوَسْوِسُ بِهِۦ نَفْسُهُۥ ۖ وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ ٱلْوَرِيدِ ‎⟫
> *And indeed We have created man, and We know what his ownself whispers to him. And We are nearer to him than his jugular vein (by Our Knowledge).*
> And We have certainly created man and We know what his soul whispers to him; We are nearer to him than his jugular vein.
> — **Quran 50:16 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 112:4⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُۥ كُفُوًا أَحَدٌۢ ‎⟫
> *"And there is none co-equal or comparable unto Him." *
> And there is none equal to Him.
> — **Quran 112:4 (inline widget)**

Nothing can stand apart from God, yet nothing has any likeness with Him. He is at once entered into every being and withdrawn from every being. His being with all things and in all things does not diminish His divinity; His being apart from all things does not lift Him out of them. Mawlānā ʿAlī said: God is one — not by number, but in the sense that He has no equal. What is unique cannot be counted; to say God can be counted is *shirk* (associationism). He makes clear that no existent has being apart from His being. He is the sole existent; everything else is illusion. The radical concept of oneness that excludes all otherness in fact declares that any creation taken to subsist *alongside* God is a false god, for it then appears as another "one" set against the singularity of the One.

All praise belongs to God who is present in the hidden essence of things and whose essence is manifest through the existence of His creatures. The eye that sees cannot endure Him, yet the eye that does not see Him cannot deny His reality; nor can the heart that acknowledges His reality contain His sight. He is so high that nothing rises higher, and so near that nothing comes nearer. He has not made the intellect capable of grasping His attributes, yet He has not held them away from its knowledge.[^cite-53]

On one hand we cannot grasp His attributes; on the other, knowledge of Him is possible. How is this paradox sustained? Through it we arrive at another dimension of *dhikr* — that it is at once to remember and to *re-cognise*. The human being is, as it were, reassembling components of knowledge that already lay in consciousness but had been forgotten. One re-cognises what was forgotten, and so comes to know it again. This does not mean that knowledge is in fragments and must be patched together into a picture; it is rather a waking, an enlightening, after the clouds of innate forgetfulness have been swept away.

Mawlānā ʿAlī says in his first sermon: ⟪ar-quote:اول الدین معرفتہ⟫ — "The first of religion is the knowledge of Him." The *first* means the foremost — taken also in the sense of beginning and foundation. The word *maʿrifa* carries the sense not only of acquired learning but of recognition. This shows that God has already placed the knowledge of the truth within human consciousness; through *dhikr* one comes to recognise it and to acknowledge anew what one had already known and forgotten. In the same sermon he says:

⟪ar-quote:فَبَعَثَ فِيهمْ رُسُلَهُ، وَوَاتَرَ إِلَيْهِمْ أَنْبِياءَهُ، لِيَسْتَأْدُوهُمْ مِيثَاقَ فِطْرَتِهِ، وَيُذَكِّرُوهُمْ مَنْسِيَّ نِعْمَتِهِ، وَيَحْتَجُّوا عَلَيْهِمْ بَالتَّبْلِيغِ، وَيُثِيرُوا لَهُمْ دَفَائِنَ الْعُقُولِ، وَيُرُوهُمْ آيَاتِ الْمَقْدِرَةِ⟫

"God raised His messengers among them and sent His prophets in succession, that they might collect from them the covenant of their primordial nature, remind them of the blessing they had forgotten, establish the proof against them through proclamation, stir up for them the buried treasures of intellects (*dafāʾin al-ʿuqūl*), and show them the signs of His power."[^cite-54]

These buried treasures are the knowledge that God has set in the depths of human consciousness — the knowledge to which God Himself summoned testimony at the moment of creation:

> ⟪quran 7:172⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَإِذْ أَخَذَ رَبُّكَ مِنۢ بَنِىٓ آدَمَ مِن ظُهُورِهِمْ ذُرِّيَّتَهُمْ وَأَشْهَدَهُمْ عَلَىٰٓ أَنفُسِهِمْ أَلَسْتُ بِرَبِّكُمْ ۖ قَالُوا۟ بَلَىٰ ۛ شَهِدْنَآ ۛ أَن تَقُولُوا۟ يَوْمَ ٱلْقِيَمَةِ إِنَّا كُنَّا عَنْ هَذَا غَفِلِينَ ‎⟫
> *And (remember) when your Lord brought forth from the Children of Adam, from their loins, their seed (or from Adam's loin his offspring) and made them testify as to themselves (saying): "Am I not your Lord?" They said: "Yes! We testify," lest you should say on the Day of Resurrection: "Verily, we have been unaware of this." *
> And when your Lord brought forth from the Children of Adam, from their loins, their descendants, and made them testify against themselves, "Am I not your Lord?" they said, "Yes — we testify" — lest you should say on the Day of Resurrection, "Indeed, we were unaware of this."
> — **Quran 7:172 (inline widget)**

These divine realities may also be read as the divine breath that God blew into Adam:

> ⟪quran 32:9⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ثُمَّ سَوَّىٰهُ وَنَفَخَ فِيهِ مِن رُّوحِهِۦ ۖ وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ ٱلسَّمْعَ وَٱلْأَبْصَرَ وَٱلْأَفْـِٔدَةَ ۚ قَلِيلًۭا مَّا تَشْكُرُونَ ‎⟫
> *Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and breathed into him the soul (created by Allah for that person), and He gave you hearing (ears), sight (eyes) and hearts. Little is the thanks you give!*
> Then He proportioned him and breathed into him of His spirit, and made for you hearing, sight, and hearts. Little do you give thanks.
> — **Quran 32:9 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 2:31⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ ٱلْأَسْمَآءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُمْ عَلَى ٱلْمَلَٓئِكَةِ فَقَالَ أَنۢبِـُٔونِى بِأَسْمَآءِ هَٓؤُلَآءِ إِن كُنتُمْ صَدِقِينَ ‎⟫
> *And He taught Adam all the names (of everything) , then He showed them to the angels and said, "Tell Me the names of these if you are truthful." *
> And He taught Adam the names — all of them — then presented them to the angels and said, "Inform Me of the names of these, if you are truthful."
> — **Quran 2:31 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 30:30⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فَأَقِمْ وَجْهَكَ لِلدِّينِ حَنِيفًۭا ۚ فِطْرَتَ ٱللَّهِ ٱلَّتِى فَطَرَ ٱلنَّاسَ عَلَيْهَا ۚ لَا تَبْدِيلَ لِخَلْقِ ٱللَّهِ ۚ ذَلِكَ ٱلدِّينُ ٱلْقَيِّمُ وَلَكِنَّ أَكْثَرَ ٱلنَّاسِ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ ‎⟫
> *So set you (O Muhammad PBUH) your face towards the religion of pure Islamic Monotheism Hanifa (worship none but Allah Alone) Allah's Fitrah (i.e. Allah's Islamic Monotheism), with which He has created mankind. No change let there be in Khalq&shy;illah (i.e. the Religion of Allah Islamic Monotheism), that is the straight religion, but most of men know not. [Tafsir At&shy;Tabarî, Vol 21, Page 41]*
> So set your face upright to the religion as a true monotheist — the original disposition (*fiṭra*) of God upon which He fashioned mankind. There is no altering God's creation. That is the upright religion, but most people do not know.
> — **Quran 30:30 (inline widget)**

The verses show that the knowledge of God has been set within the true nature (*fiṭra*) of humankind — the very knowledge to which God drew their testimony at the moment of their creation. Imām al-Bāqir (peace be upon him) was asked about this *fiṭra* and replied that God created creatures upon the bond of *tawḥīd* — that there is no god but God — and they bore witness that He is our Lord. The knowledge of God is thus the very exposition of human nature. This is why, as Mawlānā ʿAlī said, the knowledge of God cannot remain concealed from the human intellect.

Yet though this knowledge has been set eternally within the human intellect, it has been neglected — and neglect is the antithesis of *dhikr*. No measure of merely external knowledge can repair this lapse. Only when the awareness of remembrance and inner consciousness wells up from the heart and joins with outward revelation is the divine knowledge unveiled to heart and intellect alike — those treasures of knowledge that lay buried within.

Mawlānā ʿAlī says: "Praise is for God who is known without being seen." The paradox is sharpened by his statement that through the existents (*al-mawjūdāt*) the Creator is shown to the intellect, and through those same existents He is concealed from the eyes. Here we meet the veil (*ḥijāb*) that arose with the act of creation. In one sense, all creatures are themselves ⟪ar:’حجاب‘⟫: they conceal the divine self-disclosures within them while, in the very act of their existence, bearing witness to the existence of their Creator. This is why he says: ⟪ar-quote:لا تحجبہ الحجب⟫ — "Veils do not conceal Him." The veil is compelled to reveal its observer, for it discloses to him its own existence. And the Master of the Universe said: ⟪ar-quote:لو کشف الغطاء ما ازدت یقینا!⟫ — "Were the veil to be lifted, my certainty would not increase!" Yet such vision is granted only to the heart purified by *dhikr* — the heart now able to see after blindness. He then deepens the paradox: "The veil between Him and His creatures is their very createdness, that the Creator may be distinguished from the created, the infinite from the finite, the Lord from the served."

These creatures, these veils woven by creation, are themselves instances of the divine attribute *al-Ẓāhir* — manifest, seen, evident, visible. They give voice to the divine reality of ⟪ar:’خالق‘⟫ (the Creator) by which they exist. That is, God brings forth a portion of His hidden treasures as a manifest creation; He shows the outward face of the world ⟪ar:’الظاہر‘⟫ while concealing within this very expression His inner mystery ⟪ar:’الباطن‘⟫. The veil of the creature is therefore, in truth, the manifestation of the Creator. What, then, do these outward veils conceal? The very oneness of God — for ⟪ar:’الحق‘⟫ (the Real) is the foundation of their essence and ground. As noted earlier, the divine attributes are the basis of every creation. This shows that the ground of all things is a single Truth; the essence of creation is, in reality, the divine attributes of the Real. To make a creature the partner of God is to fall into *shirk*. The vision of divine oneness guards us against it. Hence the distinction must always be maintained between Creator and creature, finite and infinite, Lord and the served.

The veil of creation guards nature from pantheism, and from reducing God's infinite divinity to the limited forms of the human being. Likewise *tanzīh* (the disengagement of God from all attributes resembling created things) and *tashbīh* (the affirmation of His attributes) are themselves veils — yet veils that disclose what they conceal when seen in the light of one another. Transcendence and immanence remain veils only as long as these two opposing aspects are held apart; in the spiritual vision of ⟪ar:’توحید‘⟫ — beheld together in the creature (the manifestation of the manifest) and the non-creature (above all manifestation) — each reveals the other. To stress one-sidedly the attribute of God's manifestation turns it into a veil, for it conceals the attribute of His inner reality; insisting only on the inner aspect renders Him invisible to us, hiding the *ẓāhir* of God that dwells in the bosom of His universe. The veils, in other words, are not only the consequence of creation; they are also the consequence of the weakness and limits of human concepts, and of the limits within which the divine attributes can be made manifest. Hence the necessity of negating those attributes; through that negation consciousness becomes able to grasp the divinity and begins to see beyond the veil that cannot, in the end, conceal Him.

When Abū Saʿīd al-Kharrāz (a Sufi) was asked, "How did you know God?", he answered: by His conjoining the opposites — ⟪ar:جَمعُہُ الضِّدَّین⟫. Then he recited:

> ⟪quran 57:3⟫
> ⟪ar:‏هُوَ ٱلْأَوَّلُ وَٱلْآخِرُ وَٱلظَّهِرُ وَٱلْبَاطِنُ ۖ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَىْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ‎⟫
> *He is the First (nothing is before Him) and the Last (nothing is after Him), the Most High (nothing is above Him) and the Most Near (nothing is nearer than Him). And He is the All-Knower of every thing.*
> He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden, and He is, of every thing, the Knowing.
> — **Quran 57:3 (inline widget)**

Spiritual inspiration enables a person to behold apparently different objects as one in the light of *tawḥīd*. At this depth of consciousness the work of reintegration begins; from it the knowledge of the Real unfolds, and the heart begins to see God through the sight of the truth of faith.[^cite-55] Only the realities can have such majesty — realities concealed in the depths of faith. Only the heart that has received the remembrance of God can see and grasp them — those very realities that ground the reality of God.

[^cite-51]: Compare al-Sijistānī, *Kitāb al-Yanābīʿ*, where the work of *dhikr* and recitation is treated as the means by which the soul receives the impressions of the higher world and translates intellectual realities into a settled condition of the heart.
[^cite-52]: On the heart as the seat of insight whose purification by *dhikr* allows the inner light of the soul to be joined with the outward act of remembrance, see al-Sijistānī, *Kashf al-Maḥjūb*, in his discussion of the conditions of spiritual knowledge.
[^cite-53]: This stretch of the sermon — God known without being seen, manifest in creation yet beyond grasp — echoes the opening of Imām ʿAlī's *Khuṭba* in *Nahj al-Balāgha*, where the divine attributes are at once affirmed and held beyond the reach of created intellects.
[^cite-54]: Imām ʿAlī, sermon 1, in al-Sharīf al-Raḍī, *Nahj al-Balāgha*, on the sending of prophets to draw forth "the buried treasures of intellects" (*dafāʾin al-ʿuqūl*).
[^cite-55]: Al-Muʾayyad fī al-Dīn al-Shīrāzī, *al-Majālis al-Muʾayyadiyya*, develops the theme that the inner sight of *tawḥīd* is the harmonising of apparent opposites — *ẓāhir* and *bāṭin*, transcendence and proximity — under the single light of the Real.

<!-- section 22 (id=3603, raw_sort=22): ذکر اللہ عبادت کی معراج ہے -->
## The Remembrance of God is the Ascension of Worship

God Almighty has commanded the believer to keep His Name continually upon the tongue and within the heart. The Qurʾān returns to this command with striking frequency, framing dhikr (remembrance of God) as both an inward disposition and an outward discipline:

> ⟪quran 76:25⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱذْكُرِ ٱسْمَ رَبِّكَ بُكْرَةًۭ وَأَصِيلًۭا ‎⟫
> *And remember the Name of your Lord every morning and afternoon [i.e. offering of the Morning (Fajr), Zuhr, and 'Asr prayers].*
> and remember the name of your Lord morning and evening.
> — **Quran 76:25 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 73:8⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱذْكُرِ ٱسْمَ رَبِّكَ وَتَبَتَّلْ إِلَيْهِ تَبْتِيلًۭا ‎⟫
> *And remember the Name of your Lord and devote yourself to Him with a complete devotion.*
> And pronounce the name of your Lord and devote yourselves to Him.
> — **Quran 73:8 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 33:41⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ ٱذْكُرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ ذِكْرًۭا كَثِيرًۭا ‎⟫
> *O you who believe! Remember Allah with much remembrance.*
> O you who believe! Remember Allah with much remembrance.
> — **Quran 33:41 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 7:55⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱدْعُوا۟ رَبَّكُمْ تَضَرُّعًۭا وَخُفْيَةً ۚ إِنَّهُۥ لَا يُحِبُّ ٱلْمُعْتَدِينَ ‎⟫
> *Invoke your Lord with humility and in secret.&nbsp;He likes not the aggressors.*
> Pray to your Lord in humility and in secret. He does not love the transgressors.
> — **Quran 7:55 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 7:205⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱذْكُر رَّبَّكَ فِى نَفْسِكَ تَضَرُّعًۭا وَخِيفَةًۭ وَدُونَ ٱلْجَهْرِ مِنَ ٱلْقَوْلِ بِٱلْغُدُوِّ وَٱلْآصَالِ وَلَا تَكُن مِّنَ ٱلْغَفِلِينَ ‎⟫
> *And remember your Lord by your tongue and within yourself, humbly and with fear without loudness in words in the mornings, and in the afternoons and be not of those who are neglectful.*
> And remember your Lord in your heart, humble and fearful. And do not be of the loud voice, morning and evening, and do not be of the heedless.
> — **Quran 7:205 (inline widget)**

These verses lay down the modalities of dhikr: it is to be performed inwardly, in humility and awe, in solitude and in a lowered voice. But beyond every modality, what the Qurʾān most insistently demands is frequency. The intensity and constancy with which the Prophet and Imam ʿAlī engaged in dhikr is set out at length in Sūrat al-Muzzammil:

> ⟪quran 73:20⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ إِنَّ رَبَّكَ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّكَ تَقُومُ أَدْنَىٰ مِن ثُلُثَىِ ٱلَّيْلِ وَنِصْفَهُۥ وَثُلُثَهُۥ وَطَآئِفَةٌۭ مِّنَ ٱلَّذِينَ مَعَكَ ۚ وَٱللَّهُ يُقَدِّرُ ٱلَّيْلَ وَٱلنَّهَارَ ۚ عَلِمَ أَن لَّن تُحْصُوهُ فَتَابَ عَلَيْكُمْ ۖ فَٱقْرَءُوا۟ مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ ٱلْقُرْآنِ ۚ عَلِمَ أَن سَيَكُونُ مِنكُم مَّرْضَىٰ ۙ وَآخَرُونَ يَضْرِبُونَ فِى ٱلْأَرْضِ يَبْتَغُونَ مِن فَضْلِ ٱللَّهِ ۙ وَآخَرُونَ يُقَتِلُونَ فِى سَبِيلِ ٱللَّهِ ۖ فَٱقْرَءُوا۟ مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنْهُ ۚ وَأَقِيمُوا۟ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ وَآتُوا۟ ٱلزَّكَوٰةَ وَأَقْرِضُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ قَرْضًا حَسَنًۭا ۚ وَمَا تُقَدِّمُوا۟ لِأَنفُسِكُم مِّنْ خَيْرٍۢ تَجِدُوهُ عِندَ ٱللَّهِ هُوَ خَيْرًۭا وَأَعْظَمَ أَجْرًۭا ۚ وَٱسْتَغْفِرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ ۖ إِنَّ ٱللَّهَ غَفُورٌۭ رَّحِيمٌۢ ‎⟫
> *Verily, your Lord knows that you do stand (to pray at night) a little less than two-thirds of the night, or half the night, or a third of the night, and so do a party of those with you, And Allah measures the night and the day. He knows that you are unable to pray the whole night, so He has turned to you (in mercy). So, recite you of the Quran as much as may be easy for you. He knows that there will be some among you sick, others travelling through the land, seeking of Allah's Bounty; yet others fighting in Allah's Cause. So recite as much of the Quran as may be easy (for you), and perform As-Salat (Iqamat-as-Salat) and give Zakat, and lend to Allah a goodly loan, and whatever good you send before you for yourselves, (i.e. Nawafil non-obligatory acts of worship: prayers, charity, fasting, Hajj and 'Umrah, etc.), you will certainly find it with Allah,  better and greater in reward. And seek Forgiveness of Allah. Verily, Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most-Merciful.*
> your Lord knows that you and those with you stand near two-thirds of the night, and at half-night, and He deems the night and the day. He knows that you cannot recite it, so He has mercy on you. "Some of you will be sick, and there will be some who will travel in the land seeking the bounty of Allah, and some will be those who strive in the way of Allah, so perform the prayer that is easy thereof, and perform the prayer, and pay the poor-rate, and lend to Allah in a goodly manner, and whatever good you send forward for yourselves, it is with Allah." And you will have a better reward and a greater reward, and ask forgiveness of Allah; surely Allah is Forgiving, Merciful.
> — **Quran 73:20 (inline widget)**

The opening verses of the same sūra strike the same note, exhorting the Messenger directly:

> ⟪quran 73:8⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱذْكُرِ ٱسْمَ رَبِّكَ وَتَبَتَّلْ إِلَيْهِ تَبْتِيلًۭا ‎⟫
> *And remember the Name of your Lord and devote yourself to Him with a complete devotion.*
> And pronounce the name of your Lord and devote yourselves to Him.
> — **Quran 73:8 (inline widget)**

The word *ism* ("name") here carries a double sense: it is at once that by which the Lord is invoked and that through which He becomes intelligible to the worshipper. The verse therefore enjoins the believer to call upon His Name and to withdraw from all others in His direction. In this sense, devotion to the Name of God is itself devotion to God Most High. God in His essence transcends every utterance and every form of speech, yet He has made Himself accessible through His Names — Names that no human being has fabricated, but which He Himself has chosen for Himself. To remain ignorant of these Names is, in effect, to remain indifferent to God Himself, as the Qurʾān warns:

> ⟪quran 58:19⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱسْتَحْوَذَ عَلَيْهِمُ ٱلشَّيْطَنُ فَأَنسَىٰهُمْ ذِكْرَ ٱللَّهِ ۚ أُو۟لَٓئِكَ حِزْبُ ٱلشَّيْطَنِ ۚ أَلَآ إِنَّ حِزْبَ ٱلشَّيْطَنِ هُمُ ٱلْخَسِرُونَ ‎⟫
> *&nbsp;Shaitan (Satan) has overtaken them. So he has made them forget the remembrance of Allah. They are the party of Shaitan (Satan). Verily, it is the party of Shaitan (Satan) that will be the losers!*
> Satan has got the better of them, and he has made them forget the remembrance of Allah. They are Satan's faction. Unquestionably, Satan's party will be the losers.
> — **Quran 58:19 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 63:9⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ لَا تُلْهِكُمْ أَمْوَلُكُمْ وَلَآ أَوْلَدُكُمْ عَن ذِكْرِ ٱللَّهِ ۚ وَمَن يَفْعَلْ ذَلِكَ فَأُو۟لَٓئِكَ هُمُ ٱلْخَسِرُونَ ‎⟫
> *O you who believe! Let not your properties or your children divert you from the remembrance of Allah. And whosoever does that, then they are the losers.*
> O you who believe! Let not your wealth or your children divert you from the remembrance of Allah. And whoever does that, then those! they are the losers.
> — **Quran 63:9 (inline widget)**

The intimate bond between love of God and remembrance of His Names is captured in a saying of Imam ʿAlī: ⟪ar-quote:من أحب شئی لھج بذکرہ⟫ — "Whoever loves a thing keeps mentioning it ardently." The verb *lahija*, of which ⟪ar:لھج⟫ is the root, denotes both "to dedicate oneself" and "to repeat over and over again." The believer, then, should keep the Name of God upon his tongue and within his heart in unceasing repetition, as the Prophet implores in the Duʿāʾ al-Kāmil: ⟪ar-quote:لھِجَ بہ لسانِ من ذکرک⟫ — "May the tongue of one who remembers You be devoted to Your remembrance"; and again: ⟪ar-quote:عِج الِّساَنِ بِذِکرِکَ لَھِجاً⟫ — "Make my tongue ardently devoted to Your remembrance." In a further supplication he names God thus: ⟪ar-quote:یا من اسمہ دواؤٌ و ذکرہ شفاؤٌ⟫ — "O Thou whose Name is the medicine and whose remembrance is the cure."

The "Name of God," then, is not a mere word in the manner of other names. It is the remedy for an illness inscribed in the very birth of the human being — the chronic forgetfulness that has become his second nature. Yet, as with any medicine, contemplation alone does not heal: thinking about the Names of God, however serious, is insufficient unless the Names are actually invoked. It is for this reason that God calls the Qurʾān itself a "remembrance" (*dhikr*) and commands frequent recitation of it. The Prophet, likewise, presented remembrance as a concrete act. ʿAbd Allāh ibn Busr narrates that a man came to the Messenger of God and said, "O Messenger of God, the laws of Islām have become many for me; tell me one thing to which I may cling." The Prophet replied: "Let your tongue never cease to be moist with the remembrance of God." The Qurʾānic verse below confirms the principle:

> ⟪quran 33:41-43⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ ٱذْكُرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ ذِكْرًۭا كَثِيرًۭا ‎ ‏وَسَبِّحُوهُ بُكْرَةًۭ وَأَصِيلًا ‎ ‏هُوَ ٱلَّذِى يُصَلِّى عَلَيْكُمْ وَمَلَٓئِكَتُهُۥ لِيُخْرِجَكُم مِّنَ ٱلظُّلُمَتِ إِلَى ٱلنُّورِۚ وَكَانَ بِٱلْمُؤْمِنِينَ رَحِيمًۭا ‎⟫
> *O you who believe! Remember Allah with much remembrance. And glorify His Praises morning and afternoon [the early morning (Fajr) and 'Asr prayers]. He it is Who sends Salat (His blessings) on you, and His angels too (ask Allah to bless and forgive you), that He may bring you out from darkness (of disbelief and polytheism) into light (of Belief and Islamic Monotheism). And He is Ever Most Merciful to the believers.*
> O you who believe! Remember Allah much, and glorify Him morning and evening. It is He who sends His mercy upon you, and His angels, that He may bring you out of darkness into the light, and He is Most Merciful to the believers.
> — **Quran 33:41-43 (inline widget)**

The conjunction is significant: the dhikr of the believers, the angelic prayer (*ṣalāt*) of God Himself, and the movement from darkness into light are bound together in a single economy of grace. The reciprocity that lies at its heart is then made explicit:

> ⟪quran 2:152⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فَٱذْكُرُونِىٓ أَذْكُرْكُمْ وَٱشْكُرُوا۟ لِى وَلَا تَكْفُرُونِ ‎⟫
> *Therefore remember Me (by praying, glorifying, etc.). I will remember you, and be grateful to Me (for My countless Favours on you) and never be ungrateful to Me.*
> So remember Me, I will remember you, and be grateful to Me, and do not be ungrateful.
> — **Quran 2:152 (inline widget)**

Imam ʿAlī, in one of his sermons, declares that whoever remembers God, God revives his heart and illuminates his inner substance (*lubb*).[^cite-56] The image is precise and Qurʾānic: the same word *lubb* lies behind the Qurʾān's recurrent address to *ūlū al-albāb* — "those who possess substance," those whose inner faculty has not been hollowed out by heedlessness:

> ⟪quran 3:190-191⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّ فِى خَلْقِ ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ وَٱخْتِلَفِ ٱلَّيْلِ وَٱلنَّهَارِ لَآيَتٍۢ لِّأُو۟لِى ٱلْأَلْبَبِ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ يَذْكُرُونَ ٱللَّهَ قِيَمًۭا وَقُعُودًۭا وَعَلَىٰ جُنُوبِهِمْ وَيَتَفَكَّرُونَ فِى خَلْقِ ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ رَبَّنَا مَا خَلَقْتَ هَذَا بَطِلًۭا سُبْحَنَكَ فَقِنَا عَذَابَ ٱلنَّارِ ‎⟫
> *Verily! In the creation of the heavens and the earth, and in the alternation of night and day, there are indeed signs for men of understanding. Those who remember Allah (always, and in prayers) standing, sitting, and lying down on their sides, and think deeply about the creation of the heavens and the earth, (saying): "Our Lord! You have not created (all) this without purpose, glory to You! (Exalted be You above all that they associate with You as partners). Give us salvation from the torment of the Fire.*
> in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and in the alternation of night and day, there are signs for men of understanding, those who remember Allah standing, sitting, and lying on their sides, and reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth, saying: Our Lord! Thou hast not created this for vain; exalted be He above all faults; therefore save us from the chastisement of the Fire.
> — **Quran 3:190-191 (inline widget)**

This verse makes plain the inner correlation between remembrance and reflection. The act of dhikr quickens the heart; the quickened heart sharpens consciousness; sharpened consciousness deepens contemplation of the signs of God in creation and in the self. When love is woven into this contemplation — for, as the Prophet said, "whoever loves a thing keeps mentioning it ardently" — a circle of faith is formed in which each part reinforces every other. The more seriously the believer contemplates the Names of God, the more he perceives their beauty, and the more he loves God; the more he loves God, the more frequently he invokes His Names; and the more he invokes His Names, the more deeply the secrets concealed in God's signs disclose themselves to him. Thought leads to an increase of remembrance, and remembrance to an increase of thought.[^cite-57]

Dhikr is, in this sense, the pinnacle (*miʿrāj*) of all acts of worship. The ritual acts — standing, bowing, prostration, silent inwardness, fearful humility, the lowered voice — each have their place, but each also has its time, its place, and its conditions. Dhikr, by contrast, knows none of these bounds. It may occur at any hour, in every dwelling, in every state. It is therefore not an act standing apart from the rest of life, like the prescribed rites, but a discipline that must be woven through the whole of one's daily routine. Imam Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq said: "For every act there is a limit at which it ends — save the remembrance of God."
