God has made certain religious duties incumbent upon humankind, and the one who fulfils them reaches their appointed limits. Thus whoever fasts the thirty days of Ramaḍān has reached the limit of fasting; whoever performs the Ḥajj rites in the sacred precincts of God has fulfilled their measure. Yet for dhikr there is no such terminus. Imam Muḥammad al-Bāqir is reported to have remembered God even while eating and walking.[^cite-58]

It is in this light that the Qurʾān's praise of those whom neither trade nor merchandise distract from the remembrance of God becomes intelligible. These are the people whose entire condition is one of unbroken prayer:

> ⟪quran 70:23⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ عَلَىٰ صَلَاتِهِمْ دَآئِمُونَ ‎⟫
> *Those who remain constant in their Salat (prayers);*
> who are steadfast in their prayers.
> — **Quran 70:23 (inline widget)**

And God Himself equates ritual prayer with His own remembrance:

> ⟪quran 20:14⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّنِىٓ أَنَا ٱللَّهُ لَآ إِلَهَ إِلَّآ أَنَا۠ فَٱعْبُدْنِى وَأَقِمِ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ لِذِكْرِىٓ ‎⟫
> *"Verily! I am Allah! La ilaha illa Ana (none has the right to be worshipped but I), so worship Me, and perform As&shy;Salat (Iqamat-as-Salat) for My Remembrance.*
> I am God; there is no god but I, so worship Me and establish prayer for My remembrance.
> — **Quran 20:14 (inline widget)**

Imam ʿAlī declares that the unceasing remembrance of God is the sustenance of the soul and the very key of worship — *miftāḥ al-ṣalāt*.

> ⟪quran 29:45⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱتْلُ مَآ أُوحِىَ إِلَيْكَ مِنَ ٱلْكِتَبِ وَأَقِمِ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ ۖ إِنَّ ٱلصَّلَوٰةَ تَنْهَىٰ عَنِ ٱلْفَحْشَآءِ وَٱلْمُنكَرِ ۗ وَلَذِكْرُ ٱللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ مَا تَصْنَعُونَ ‎⟫
> *Recite (O Muhammad PBUH) what has been revealed to you of the Book (the Quran), and perform As-Salat (Iqamat&shy;as&shy;Salat). Verily, As-Salat (the prayer) prevents from Al-Fahsha' (i.e. great sins of every kind, unlawful sexual intercourse, etc.) and Al-Munkar (i.e. disbelief, polytheism, and every kind of evil wicked deed, etc.) and the remembering (praising, etc.) of (you by) Allah (in front of the angels) is greater indeed [than your remembering (praising, etc.) Allah in prayers, etc.]. And Allah knows what you do.*
> recite what has been revealed to you of the Book, and be steadfast in prayer. Indeed, prayer prevents indecency and evil, and the remembrance of Allah is greater; and Allah is Aware of what you do.
> — **Quran 29:45 (inline widget)**

Here the ritual prayer is described as a preventive — a guard against indecency and against every form of *munkar*. Yet for all its appointed times, postures, and gestures, *ṣalāt* remains a delimited rite. Dhikr is greater: it is the living substance that animates the rite, and is itself unconfined by such limitations or impurities. The relation of dhikr to *ṣalāt* is, in this sense, like the relation of the soul to the body. The soul does not negate the body; rather, it encompasses it, vivifies it, and brings it through the body to wholeness and perfection.

<!-- section 23 (id=3604, raw_sort=23): ذکر اللہ کی حقیقت -->
## The Reality of Dhikr Allāh

We return now to the fruit of dhikr Allāh (the remembrance of God) and to its principal aim: realisation — the process by which the truth becomes real for the one who remembers. ⟪ar:تَوَحٌُد⟫ This is akin to the integration of the self with the self, an assimilation that differs from the monotheism which pairs one with another. The fruit of dhikr is best grasped through its contraries: heedlessness, forgetfulness, distraction.

God's condemnation of negligent and ostentatious prayer is sharp:

> ⟪quran 107:4-6⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فَوَيْلٌۭ لِّلْمُصَلِّينَ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ عَن صَلَاتِهِمْ سَاهُونَ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ يُرَآءُونَ ‎⟫
> *So woe unto those performers of Salat (prayers) (hypocrites), Who delay their Salat (prayer) from their stated fixed times, Those who do good deeds only to be seen (of men),*
> woe to those who pray who are heedless of their prayers, and who show off.
> — **Quran 107:4-6 (inline widget)**

In the same spirit, Imam ʿAlī forbids the mention of God carried out in negligence or pretence. Yet when the rememberer's self-regard is purged from the act, dhikr becomes perfect — a remembrance in which the rememberer forgets himself. The Imam holds together two contraries: that no negligence may enter the dhikr, and that one's attention must be so absolute that the self becomes oblivious to itself, attaining the state of ⟪ar:’الفنا فی الذکر‘⟫ — fanāʾ fī al-dhikr (annihilation in the remembrance). He says:

> Do not remember God carelessly, nor forget Him in your distraction; rather remember Him fully — a remembrance in which there is harmony between your heart and your tongue, and what you conceal is consistent with what you display. But you will never be able to remember Him in truth until you become heedless of your own soul in this remembrance.

This saying is the very kernel of Sufism. It gathers into one image every place from which perfect sincerity (ikhlāṣ) is born: the second name of Sufism is honesty and ikhlāṣ. On one side, dhikr quickens the heart, and from that quickening sincerity arises; on the other, sincerity is the virtue that brings dhikr to its consummation. In this perfected place, the utterance of the tongue agrees entirely with the intention of the heart, and not the slightest seam remains between word and intent. The concord of tongue and heart means that the divine reality released upon the tongue becomes the echo of the heart's glorification — the heart that mirrors innate nature (fiṭra) and is the workshop in which all spiritual virtues are produced.

When the soul becomes wholly purified — when intellect and tongue speak as one — it grows transparent to the spiritual standards embedded within it, and the rememberer begins to see the treasures buried in his own depths. These are the standards that take fire in the heart by the remembrance of God, and then manifest outwardly in conduct. Without these moral and spiritual standards, the outward act of dhikr is negligent and unfit; the ignorant one is he who shuns the striving for sincerity, turns from the greater jihād (al-jihād al-akbar), and abstains from carrying out God's command.[^cite-59]

Imam ʿAlī's saying opens the gate of dhikr's reality, through which we may move toward al-Ḥaqq (the Real). To carry out remembrance at its highest pitch, fanāʾ al-nafs (annihilation of the self, self-effacement) is required. To behold the connection, one must remove the veil of selfhood that stands between the truth and oneself. The beginning of knowledge is the effacement of the self; its extremity is the annihilation of the self. The reality of dhikr therefore demands a revolutionary heedlessness — heedlessness toward all that is not the Remembered. By it the human being moves from perfection toward the reality of the latter, from human perfection toward divine reality (al-Ḥaqq). The first stage is the elimination of pretence and the pursuit of sincerity, honesty, and integrity that yield human perfection; here the individual's own identity persists. This harmony of intention and action is what is called perfection. Yet because remembrance is still mediated through the human being himself, it remains of lower rank. In reality, the self's identity does not endure, for it has grown oblivious to itself. At this stage ⟪ar:’ذاکر‘⟫ — the dhākir (the rememberer) — gives way entirely to ⟪ar:’مذکور‘⟫ — the madhkūr (the Remembered). The reality of dhikr becomes one with the truth. Inwardly it may then be said that the One who mentions the Truth is God Himself, while outwardly it is issued upon the tongue of the human being. At that moment, the human being's seeming existence is a veil that the very act of remembering effaces.

In this light we return to the ḥadīth qudsī: *My servant does not cease to draw near to Me through nawāfil (supererogatory acts of devotion) until I love him.* We see now that by *nawāfil* what is intended is the remembrance of God. It is striking that God is the root of every action of the dhākir: He sees and hears the deeds of those whom He loves. God does not alter Himself to enter the intentions and actions of a human being; rather He becomes one with that person's intention and action. In other words, divine realities veil themselves beneath the dhākir's soul, which enables him to see past the suspicion of his outward existence and to perceive the inner truths of the Qurʾān:

> ⟪quran 37:96⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ‎⟫
> *"While Allah has created you and what you make!" *
> God has created you and what you make.
> — **Quran 37:96 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 8:17⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فَلَمْ تَقْتُلُوهُمْ وَلَكِنَّ ٱللَّهَ قَتَلَهُمْ ۚ وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ ٱللَّهَ رَمَىٰ ۚ وَلِيُبْلِىَ ٱلْمُؤْمِنِينَ مِنْهُ بَلَآءً حَسَنًا ۚ إِنَّ ٱللَّهَ سَمِيعٌ عَلِيمٌۭ ‎⟫
> *You killed them not, but Allah killed them. And you (Muhammad PBUH) threw not when you did throw but Allah threw, that He might test the believers by a fair trial from Him. Verily, Allah is All-Hearer, All-Knower.*
> It was not you who killed them, but it was God who killed them. And you did not throw your handful when you threw, but it was God who threw, and that He might bestow His grace on the believers. God is All-hearing, All-knowing.
> — **Quran 8:17 (inline widget)**

This is the very point that the Prophet's remembrance and Imam ʿAlī's remembrance illustrated: in a battle an arrow pierced the Imam's ankle, and he instructed the companions, "When I have entered prostration in prayer, draw it out." So it was done while he was in sajda; and on completing the prayer he asked, "Have you removed the arrow?" — as though he had not even noticed. This is the apex of remembrance, where the physical existence of the human being (and the sensations attached to it) no longer registers.

And as the Messenger of God said, *iḥsān is to worship God as though you see Him; and if you do not see Him, He sees you.* The Arabic of this ḥadīth is such that if one pauses at a different point, the sense is altered: *if you do not see Him, He sees you* — for only God can behold the vision of God, not the servant. The true theophany cannot be attained so long as any vestige of creaturely consciousness remains in the gaze of the seeker. As in the story of Moses:

> ⟪quran 7:143⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَمَّا جَآءَ مُوسَىٰ لِمِيقَتِنَا وَكَلَّمَهُۥ رَبُّهُۥ قَالَ رَبِّ أَرِنِىٓ أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ۚ قَالَ لَن تَرَىٰنِى وَلَكِنِ ٱنظُرْ إِلَى ٱلْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ ٱسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُۥ فَسَوْفَ تَرَىٰنِى ۚ فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّىٰ رَبُّهُۥ لِلْجَبَلِ جَعَلَهُۥ دَكًّۭا وَخَرَّ مُوسَىٰ صَعِقًۭا ۚ فَلَمَّآ أَفَاقَ قَالَ سُبْحَنَكَ تُبْتُ إِلَيْكَ وَأَنَا۠ أَوَّلُ ٱلْمُؤْمِنِينَ ‎⟫
> *And when Musa (Moses) came at the time and place appointed by Us, and his Lord spoke to him, he said: "O my Lord! Show me (Yourself), that I may look upon You." Allah said: "You cannot see Me, but look upon the mountain if it stands still in its place then you shall see Me." So when his Lord appeared to the mountain, He made it collapse to dust, and Musa (Moses) fell down unconscious. Then when he recovered his senses he said: "Glory be to You, I turn to You in repentance and I am the first of the believers." *
> And when Moses came to Our appointed time, and his Lord spoke to them, he said, "My Lord, show me that I may look at You." He said, "You do not see Me, but if it stays still, then You will see Me." Then when his Lord showed light to the mountain, He made it to pieces. Then when he regained consciousness, he said, "Glory be to You! I repent to You, and I am the first of the believers."
> — **Quran 7:143 (inline widget)**

The reflection of al-Ḥaqq's radiance made Moses fall unconscious. God did not shine upon Moses; He shone upon the mountain — and even that, Moses could not bear.

Once, Abū al-Dardāʾ — a companion of the Prophet — found Imam ʿAlī lying in a Medinan garden, his body wholly motionless, as if lifeless. Alarmed, he went to the house of Fāṭima to bring her the news. She replied, "My husband ʿAlī has not died; he must have fainted from the awe of God. Such states often come upon him." The fear meant here is the knowledge of God's majesty. Of this fear of perfection the Qurʾān says:

> ⟪quran 79:40-41⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِۦ وَنَهَى ٱلنَّفْسَ عَنِ ٱلْهَوَىٰ ‎ ‏فَإِنَّ ٱلْجَنَّةَ هِىَ ٱلْمَأْوَىٰ ‎⟫
> *But as for him who feared standing before his Lord, and restrained himself from impure evil desires, and lusts. Verily, Paradise will be his abode.*
> But as for him who fears to stand before his Lord, and restrain himself from lust, his abode is Paradise.
> — **Quran 79:40-41 (inline widget)**

Though fear is ordinarily a thing of terror, this fear of God is not terrifying but beautiful. It gives the human being a power of attraction toward the truth. As the Sufis often say: when one fears a creature, one flees from it; but when one fears God, one runs toward Him — as the Qurʾān testifies:

> ⟪quran 9:118⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَعَلَى ٱلثَّلَثَةِ ٱلَّذِينَ خُلِّفُوا۟ حَتَّىٰٓ إِذَا ضَاقَتْ عَلَيْهِمُ ٱلْأَرْضُ بِمَا رَحُبَتْ وَضَاقَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ أَنفُسُهُمْ وَظَنُّوٓا۟ أَن لَّا مَلْجَأَ مِنَ ٱللَّهِ إِلَّآ إِلَيْهِ ثُمَّ تَابَ عَلَيْهِمْ لِيَتُوبُوٓا۟ ۚ إِنَّ ٱللَّهَ هُوَ ٱلتَّوَّابُ ٱلرَّحِيمُ ‎⟫
> *And (He did forgive also) the three [who did not join the Tabuk expedition (whom the Prophet PBUH)] left (i.e. he did not give his judgement in their case, and their case was suspended for Allah's Decision) till for them the earth, vast as it is, was straitened and their ownselves were straitened to them, and they perceived that there is no fleeing from Allah,  and no refuge but with Him. Then, He accepted their repentance, that they might repent (unto Him). Verily, Allah is the One Who accepts repentance, Most Merciful.*
> and to the three whose case was deferred, until when the earth, vast as it was, became straitened for them, and their souls became straitened for them, and they realized that there was no refuge from Allah, so He turned to them of His mercy, so that they might repent; surely Allah is the Acceptor of Repentance, the Merciful.
> — **Quran 9:118 (inline widget)**

Imam ʿAlī's saying intimates the spiritual love and the conscious bond with the truth that — as Fāṭima observed — would so often cast him into such a state.

Truly, God has a draught reserved for His saints. When they drink it, they are made drunk; when drunk, they are made joyful; when joyful, they are blessed; when blessed, they are dissolved; when dissolved, they are made free; when made free, they are purified. They seek, and in seeking they find (wujūd), and in finding they arrive; and on arriving they become one with the Beloved in such a way that no distinction remains between them and Him.

The Messenger of God said, "There have come upon them moments of the knowledge of perfect tawḥīd (oneness) that no angel and no prophet could bear." This taste of monotheism is the fruit of every act of worship and the keenest of all intoxications. It is this wine that the dhākir is going to drink. In the earlier stages it appears that the servant drinks the wine of the remembrance of God's names — that the servant remembers God; but at the highest degrees God Himself drinks the wine of His servant's name and begins to mention him. This occurs once the human being has annihilated his soul in the remembrance of God. Imam ʿAlī teaches that dhikr is not a manner of speech and not a manner of thought: ⟪ar:’مذکور‘⟫ — the Remembered — is first released; then ⟪ar:’ذاکر‘⟫ — the rememberer — is released a second time (⟪ar:اول من المذکور و ثانی من الذاکر⟫).

That is to say, God is prior to remembrance; the dhikr of the human being is a reflection of God's dhikr and a consequence of that divine remembrance. He says, *Remember Me, and I will remember you.* If we probe further into dhikr, then God's primordial command released through the word ⟪ar:’کُن‘⟫ ("Be!"), from which all existence was brought forth, may itself be viewed as a kind of remembrance. God uttered the name of the possible and summoned it, and so it stood forth from the depths of non-being. God was the rememberer of creation; creation became His remembrance. The Qurʾān asks:

> ⟪quran 76:1⟫
> ⟪ar:‏هَلْ أَتَىٰ عَلَى ٱلْإِنسَنِ حِينٌۭ مِّنَ ٱلدَّهْرِ لَمْ يَكُن شَيْـًۭٔا مَّذْكُورًا ‎⟫
> *Has there not been over man a period of time, when he was nothing to be mentioned?*
> man must have come upon a time when there was no mention of it
> — **Quran 76:1 (inline widget)**

Here the human being is called a "thing" that God brought into being. The verse's negation indicates a time when there was no mention of him — meaning that God had not commanded him to step out of non-being. At that time the human being was known, but only by God. The remembrance of God for the human being therefore precedes the existence of the human being, and the un-figured remembrance of God is greater than the human being. The human being mentions God by His names, while God mentions the human being by the word "Be." Yet that word was not a single instant that flashed and faded; rather it continues to resound in the human soul until the Day of Judgement, as a witness to the divinity of God Most High. Through that word God breathed His spirit into the soul of existence.[^cite-60]

Kumayl asked the Commander of the Faithful, "What is al-ḥaqīqa (the Reality)?" He replied: "Al-ḥaqīqa is the unveiling of the splendours of majesty without any indication. It is, in the light of the known, the annihilation of all that one would suppose Him to be. It is the name of total connection with the attribute of oneness. It is a light shining forth from the dawn of pre-eternity, whose rays fall upon the temple of monotheism."

By "temple," he indicates God in the locus of tawḥīd. The names give life to the soul and provide it with sustenance; the soul is the locus in which the names manifest, where God reveals Himself in a particular manner. Ibn al-ʿArabī writes in his *Futūḥāt al-Makkiyya* that all praise belongs to Him who created things from nothing, denied their nothingness, and brought them into being through the attention of His words. As a result of the divine Word turning its attention toward possible objects, the attributes of this world come into being — meaning that the spirit of possibilities is breathed into existence in relation to its body. The heart within that body is the name of God that gives it life. Every slave bears a name derived from his master; the slave becomes the flesh of the master, and the master becomes the heart of the slave.

This image reminds us of the Commander of the Faithful's saying that dhikr is a fire for the hearts. The name (of God) that descends from above is reflected in the name that resounds within the heart, and this reflection occurs only when the rust of the heart has been burnt away by the remembrance of God. It is the meeting of an outer light with an inner light — the Qurʾānic figure of *light upon light* (nūr ʿalā nūr).

A question now arises: what is the difference between the divine names that are the origin of every thing, and the names mentioned in the Qurʾān? On one hand, all creation is the Book of God and every phenomenon is a word of that Book; on the other, certain names mentioned in the Qurʾān are singled out for us to glorify. Why this distinction? The Qurʾān itself offers one answer:

> ⟪quran 17:110⟫
> ⟪ar:‏قُلِ ٱدْعُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ أَوِ ٱدْعُوا۟ ٱلرَّحْمَنَ ۖ أَيًّۭا مَّا تَدْعُوا۟ فَلَهُ ٱلْأَسْمَآءُ ٱلْحُسْنَىٰ ۚ وَلَا تَجْهَرْ بِصَلَاتِكَ وَلَا تُخَافِتْ بِهَا وَٱبْتَغِ بَيْنَ ذَلِكَ سَبِيلًۭا ‎⟫
> *Say (O Muhammad PBUH): "Invoke Allah or invoke the Most Beneficent (Allah), by whatever name you invoke Him (it is the same), for to Him belong the Best Names. And offer your Salat (prayer) neither aloud nor in a low voice, but follow a way between.*
> Say, 'Call upon Allah, or call upon the Most Merciful.' Whichever name you invoke, to Him belong the Most Beautiful Names. And do not recite in your prayer in a low voice, nor in a low voice, but seek a way in between.
> — **Quran 17:110 (inline widget)**

In this verse God Himself instructs the believers to invoke the names revealed in the Qurʾān. These names are altogether distinct from the names of idols.

> ⟪quran 53:23⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنْ هِىَ إِلَّآ أَسْمَآءٌۭ سَمَّيْتُمُوهَآ أَنتُمْ وَآبَآؤُكُم مَّآ أَنزَلَ ٱللَّهُ بِهَا مِن سُلْطَنٍ ۚ إِن يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا ٱلظَّنَّ وَمَا تَهْوَى ٱلْأَنفُسُ ۖ وَلَقَدْ جَآءَهُم مِّن رَّبِّهِمُ ٱلْهُدَىٰٓ ‎⟫
> *They are but names which you have named, you and your fathers, for which Allah has sent down no authority. They follow but a guess and that which they themselves desire, whereas there has surely come to them the Guidance from their Lord!*
> are naught but names which ye have invented, ye and your fathers, for which Allah has sent down no authority; they follow nothing but conjecture and desire, whereas there has come to them the guidance from their Lord.
> — **Quran 53:23 (inline widget)**

The names of idols are like the names of worldly phenomena that are merely self-referential, or signs unattached to any actual referent. The names of God, by contrast, stand in an indirect relation to His self-disclosure: they are the reality of the substance He created. These names therefore have not only the power to inform but the power to transform. They can disclose the greatness and reality of God in the heart and intellect of the human being. For this reason God's existence is inescapable — no existence falls outside the compass of His being. But rust upon the heart conceals His presence, even though the heart itself is His creation.

It is necessary, therefore, to purify the heart from within through the remembrance of God so that the outward substance may be unveiled to it. The divine names of God are the summit of all theophanies, encompassing every manifestation within their ambit while themselves standing above it. This inner and outer reality may be likened to light and shadow: both are disclosures of one and the same essence, yet there is no commensuration between the two. As Ibn al-ʿArabī wrote, the existence of al-Ḥaqq is a fixed light, and all creation is the shadow that follows that light.

Imam ʿAlī says in one of his prayers, "I call upon You by all the names with which You have named Yourself." That God has named Himself is itself made evident in the majesty and power of these names. And he says, "You shall never be able to remember Him until you become heedless of yourselves in the remembrance of Him." The question arises: what is meant by forgetting one's self and growing oblivious of it? It is impossible for a conscious person to be unaware of his own existence while engaged in remembrance. If a man tries to forget himself, the very effort prevents him from being oblivious. So we must take "obliviousness to oneself" here to mean the giving of complete attention to the names of God — so that no space remains in soul, breath, intellect, or being where God's remembrance is not present; only then does annihilation find its place. This capacity for self-effacement is granted by God's grace when the human being strives to scour the rust from his heart — the rust that deprives us of the vision of God's incomparable unity. To His Prophet God says, *We shall teach you in such a way that you shall never forget* — and why should he forget, since We shall cleanse his pure heart of every rust and impurity:

> ⟪quran 87:6-7⟫
> ⟪ar:‏سَنُقْرِئُكَ فَلَا تَنسَىٰٓ ‎ ‏إِلَّا مَا شَآءَ ٱللَّهُ ۚ إِنَّهُۥ يَعْلَمُ ٱلْجَهْرَ وَمَا يَخْفَىٰ ‎⟫
> *We shall make you to recite (the Quran), so you (O Muhammad (Peace be upon him)) shall not forget (it), Except what Allah, may will, He knows what is apparent and what is hidden.*
> assuredly We shall make thee recite, and thou shalt not forget, save whom Allah wills; verily He knoweth what is manifest and what is hidden.
> — **Quran 87:6-7 (inline widget)**

And He says, *Remember your Lord when you forget:*

> ⟪quran 18:24⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِلَّآ أَن يَشَآءَ ٱللَّهُ ۚ وَٱذْكُر رَّبَّكَ إِذَا نَسِيتَ وَقُلْ عَسَىٰٓ أَن يَهْدِيَنِ رَبِّى لِأَقْرَبَ مِنْ هَذَا رَشَدًۭا ‎⟫
> *Except (with the saying), "If Allah will!" And remember your Lord when you forget and say: "It may be that my Lord guides me unto a nearer way of truth than this." *
> Except that Allah wills, and remember your Lord when he forgets, and say, Perhaps my Lord will guide me to a better way than this.
> — **Quran 18:24 (inline widget)**

That is, when the human being forgets everything that is not God, only then is he capable of remembering God. If God stands at the palace of affirmation, then the human being stands in contrast at the palace of negation. When that negation (the creature) is itself negated, complete affirmation of God remains — just as negation multiplied by negation yields affirmation.

The Creator of the cosmos says, "I am the death of death." Rūmī, in his *Mathnawī*, gives Imam ʿAlī's words this voice: *I have taken up the sword for the sake of God; I am the servant of God, and I am not subject to the commands of my body. I am God's lion, not the lion of my desires; my deeds bear witness to my religion. In battle, I am the sword of God borne by the hand of God. I have lifted the burden of my self from my path; I have forgotten all other than God. God is my sun, and I am His reflection. I am the slayer of the self! I am the veil between God and His creation. I am laden with pearls of oneness like a sword adorned with jewels. I do not kill men in war — I give them life in the hereafter.*

The movement of God toward His servant is possible only after the negation of that servant's self-existence. This negation is the nothingness from which God created existence. God made the human being out of nothing; and now the human being must annihilate himself in order to find God. By denying the substance that was never truly self-standing (for the substance of human existence is borrowed and exhausted), the human being denies his own existence; and by that very negation he affirms what has always been. As He says:

> ⟪quran 55:26-27⟫
> ⟪ar:‏كُلُّ مَنْ عَلَيْهَا فَانٍۢ ‎ ‏وَيَبْقَىٰ وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ ذُو ٱلْجَلَلِ وَٱلْإِكْرَامِ ‎⟫
> *Whatsoever is on it (the earth) will perish. And the Face of your Lord full of Majesty and Honour will abide forever.*
> whosoever is on the earth will perish, and there will remain the Countenance of your Lord, the Most Majestic and the Most Majestic.
> — **Quran 55:26-27 (inline widget)**

Every creature has two faces. One is the divine face — the reality of all things; the other is the mortal face that is destined to perish. The Face of God that endures, therefore, is not only the divine reality but the reality present within every thing. God says:

> ⟪quran 28:88⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَا تَدْعُ مَعَ ٱللَّهِ إِلَهًا آخَرَ ۘ لَآ إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ ۚ كُلُّ شَىْءٍ هَالِكٌ إِلَّا وَجْهَهُۥ ۚ لَهُ ٱلْحُكْمُ وَإِلَيْهِ تُرْجَعُونَ ‎⟫
> *And invoke not any other ilah (god) along with Allah, La ilaha illa Huwa (none has the right to be worshipped but He). Everything will perish save His Face. His is the Decision, and to Him you (all) shall be returned.*
> And do not invoke any other god along with Allah; there is no God except Him; all things will perish except His Face; His is the command, and it is to Him that you will be returned.
> — **Quran 28:88 (inline widget)**

Al-Ghazālī writes that things do not perish only when they reach a particular place or time; rather they are perishing at every moment. Whatever is other than God is, in itself, sheer privation. Through the Face of God it receives subsistence from the Almighty, which we perceive in the form of existence. Its existence is not from itself but from the Face of God. The Face of God, therefore, is the Reality, and all else is illusion. Of the Sufis he writes: "No power is left in them save the remembrance of God, and they have no power left to remember themselves." When the human being arrives here, he stands at once in fanāʾ and in baqāʾ (subsistence). Yet even mortality is itself effaced, since the soul has grown heedless of itself and even of its own annihilation — for had it any sense of its own annihilation, it would still be conscious of itself.

Al-Qushayrī writes that whoever has not tasted the desolation of fanāʾ cannot receive the sustenance of the remembrance of God. By "desolation" here is meant the proximity of the self to the self, which manifests as self-praise, desire, and ego — and this proximity cannot be undone except by the remembrance of God. This is the reality of dhikr that Imam ʿAlī compressed into the single phrase: *you should forget your self in the remembrance of God.* God says:

> ⟪quran 2:198⟫
> ⟪ar:‏لَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن تَبْتَغُوا۟ فَضْلًۭا مِّن رَّبِّكُمْ ۚ فَإِذَآ أَفَضْتُم مِّنْ عَرَفَتٍۢ فَٱذْكُرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ عِندَ ٱلْمَشْعَرِ ٱلْحَرَامِ ۖ وَٱذْكُرُوهُ كَمَا هَدَىٰكُمْ وَإِن كُنتُم مِّن قَبْلِهِۦ لَمِنَ ٱلضَّآلِّينَ ‎⟫
> *There is no sin on you if you seek the Bounty of your Lord (during pilgrimage by trading, etc.). Then when you leave 'Arafat, remember Allah (by glorifying His Praises, i.e. prayers and invocations, etc.) at the Mash'ar-il-Haram. And remember Him (by invoking Allah for all good, etc.) as He has guided you, and verily, you were, before, of those who were astray.*
> it is no sin upon you to seek the bounty of your Lord. Then, when you return from 'Arafat, remember Allah at the Sacred Place, and remember Him as He has made known to you, and before that you were of the astray.
> — **Quran 2:198 (inline widget)**

Guidance here points to elevation in the degrees of remembrance. The first is the remembrance of the tongue, which is related to the self; then the remembrance of the heart, which is related to worship; then the remembrance of the inner secret, related to the insight of worship; then the remembrance of the spirit, related to worship in its purest form.

The first, ⟪ar:’ذکر السان‘⟫ — dhikr al-lisān (remembrance of the tongue) — pertains to the self; then ⟪ar:’ذکر القلب‘⟫ — dhikr al-qalb (remembrance of the heart) — pertains to the acts of worship that release God's grace; then ⟪ar:’ذکر السر‘⟫ — dhikr al-sirr (remembrance of the inner secret) — pertains to the vision of worship through which the truths of the divine attributes are unveiled; then ⟪ar:’ذکر الروح‘⟫ — dhikr al-rūḥ (remembrance of the spirit) — pertains to the contemplation of the essence in the light radiating from that vision; then ⟪ar:’ذکر الکھفی‘⟫ — dhikr al-khafī (hidden remembrance) — pertains to the beauty of the source in which duality still subsists; and finally there is the remembrance of the origin, related to the true vision in which the end of all duality is born. Imam ʿAlī says:

> Do not abandon dhikr because you do not feel God's presence within it; it is better that you should not find Him in your remembrance than that you should forget Him outright. He will draw you from negligence to vigilance, and from there to the dhikr in which all but the Remembered vanishes — and that is no hard thing for God.

Ibn ʿAjība writes in his commentary on the *Ḥikam* that the more a human being is annihilated in the Name, the further his knowledge of the Origin extends. He writes, "The name of God is the joy of my heart and the coolness of my eyes." Every essence is named, and true joy comes always from the essence of a thing, not from its name. Imam Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq said:

> The name of God is distinct from His Essence, and everything upon which His name appears is a creation, and is therefore distinct from God. Everything that can be described is a creature, and the Creator cannot be described within the limits of the name of His creation. There are ninety-nine names of God. If name and Essence were one, then each name would be a separate god. But God is the One to whom His names point; the names are distinct from Him as they are distinct from His creation.

Each name, on the one hand, refers to an essence and identifies it; on the other, the name has an identity of its own. Raḥīm, Karīm, and Sattār each identify God, yet these names also possess their own identity — Raḥīm is not Karīm, and Karīm is not Sattār. The mutual distinction of the names indicates their limitation. Though real in themselves, they become metaphorical and ephemeral in the sight of God, and their standing before Him becomes an "imagined reality." The real reality of all names refers back to the Essence, which is God. From one vantage, then, name and Essence are distinct; from another they are identical. In one sense, the name is nothing but a divine attribute of the Essence; in another, the name is a sound spoken through words. Raḥīm has an origin which is mercy, and Karīm has an origin which is generosity; so Raḥīm is the name of Mercy (essence), and Karīm the name of Generosity (essence). In this sense, name and essence are the same.

The attributes vanish into the Essence because they are one with it and have no autonomous subsistence — and this is the reality of tawḥīd. Beyond this knowledge the word of sincerity itself drops away, for every god has been absorbed into the Essence of God and effaced. The meaning of this word is: *there is no reality but the Real (the Essence).* From this vantage only ⟪ar:’اللہ‘⟫ remains, for God is the One who has no peer, and none can know Him save God.[^cite-61]

The Messenger of God is the exemplar of the perfect human being. Imam ʿAlī says, "We are the most beautiful names of God." ⟪ar:ھو علامۃ العظمی و آیاتہ الکبری⟫ — *He is the supreme sign of God and His greatest verses.* The Messenger of God said, "Whoever has seen me has seen the truth." So the Messenger, ʿAlī, and the Imams are those who manifested the divine attributes to humanity in the physicality of the human form. They are the mirrors in which the divine attributes are reflected; the brighter the light of truth upon them, the brighter the reflection in such a perfect human being.

In the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha*, Imam ʿAlī says: "The perfection of sincerity is to strip God of attributes — for every attribute bears witness to its own self-otherness. So whoever describes God has paired Him with another; whoever pairs Him has doubled Him; whoever doubles Him has divided Him into parts; and whoever divides Him into parts is ignorant of Him."

The Imam's saying here — that purifying God from His attributes is tawḥīd — does not mean that the divine attributes are non-existent. The intent is to deny the notion that these attributes are autonomous, external, or attached to the Real as something other. It denies that the attributes stand outside God or are separate from the Real Essence. God is the One whose attributes admit of no limit; what is denied is that the reality of any divine attribute can be circumscribed by the human imagination or the human lexicon. If the divine attributes are not other than the Essence, and if God's Essence is eternal and unlimited, then it follows that the attributes too are infinite. When the Essence cannot be comprehended, the attributes cannot be comprehended either.

In one sense this is to affirm the attribute; in another, to deny it. To affirm: that the attributes are not separable from the Essence. To deny: that these attributes can be confined within our thoughts and concepts. This is the very relation between the human soul and al-Ḥaqq. The human being in himself is desolate and has no proper existence; yet on the other hand he is not severed from the Real, in that he becomes oblivious to himself in worship. When the human being ceases to subsist in himself, the subsistence of God endures. Although there is much that strikes one as paradoxical here, this is the fact: the sense of the perfect existence of al-Ḥaqq is bound up with the sense of the self's non-existence.

The Prophet said, "On the Night of Ascent (Laylat al-Miʿrāj), I learned the true meaning of the names and saw them return to a single object and a single entity. The Named was what I saw, and my own existence was my own — for my journey was within me and pointed back to me, and through it I came to know that I am a pure servant in whom is not the slightest trace of divinity."

In like manner, when Imam ʿAlī describes his own greatness in his sermons, he is in truth describing the greatness of God being disclosed through him. He is the embodiment of God's greatness, and that greatness is the highest standard of self-effacement. Someone once accused Imam Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq of arrogance. He replied: "I am not arrogant; pride belongs only to God. I have completely cleansed the field of my self of arrogance; in its place stands the greatness of God. So long as selfish arrogance subsists in a place, the divine greatness cannot occupy it; and when divine greatness is present, then any 'pride' is by virtue of it." At this point the divine attributes, upon the mirror of a self made transparent by the divine realities, shine more powerfully and swiftly. And this incandescence arose when I cleansed the dust of ego and the desires ⟪ar:ھوی⟫ of self-love from the mirror of my heart.

Let us return now to the ḥadīth qudsī: *My servant draws near to Me through the obligatory acts of worship, which are dearest to Me, and never ceases to draw near to Me through the supererogatory acts (nawāfil) until I love him. And when I love him, I become his hearing by which he hears, his sight by which he sees, his hand by which he grasps, and his foot by which he walks.*

Consider its paradox. On one side, the most beloved deed of the human being in God's sight is the obligatory; on the other, it is the servant's supererogatory devotions that bring him near, until God begins to love him — a love seated in the senses and faculties of the human being and reflecting tawḥīd. The paradox dissolves once we see that when God loves the obligatory He loves something distinct from His Essence; but when He loves His servant — the servant who has grown oblivious of himself in the remembrance of Him — that love is greater, because the servant has become the locus of God's self-disclosure, having become one with Him. At that moment, God's love for the servant becomes God's love for His own Self. God willed to know Himself, and so created this world; when a human being effaces himself in the remembrance of God and the knowledge of God, the divine command — *that He know Himself* — is brought to its consummation.

Imam ʿAlī says, "It rains down from me, and no bird can fly to me." It rains down from you because you have become one with the spring of this knowledge. God says:

> ⟪quran 76:5-6⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّ ٱلْأَبْرَارَ يَشْرَبُونَ مِن كَأْسٍۢ كَانَ مِزَاجُهَا كَافُورًا ‎ ‏عَيْنًۭا يَشْرَبُ بِهَا عِبَادُ ٱللَّهِ يُفَجِّرُونَهَا تَفْجِيرًۭا ‎⟫
> *Verily, the Abrar (pious, who fear Allah and avoid evil), shall drink a cup (of wine) mixed with water from a spring in Paradise called Kafur. A spring wherefrom the slaves of Allah will drink, causing it to gush forth abundantly.*
> the righteous will drink a cup of wine wherein is a spring of camphor, a fountain from which the servants of Allah will drink, causing it to gush forth.
> — **Quran 76:5-6 (inline widget)**

This camphor-wine is in truth the very essence of God. The righteous will increase the spring of this wine because the wine is itself camphor, and no duality remains between them and it. The remembrance of God does more than scour the dust from the heart and quicken it; it erases the very name and trace of duality by the grace of the Remembered. It purifies the heart of every form of shirk (associationism) and establishes the principle of tawḥīd. To grasp this reality is to negate every other reality that seems apparent.

On one side, the human being knows God through the very fact that He cannot be known — for He stands beyond the veil of the cosmos. On the other, in the remembrance of God, when the human being grows oblivious of himself, the distinction between Creator and creature dissolves. The self at that moment does not exchange itself for anything else; rather, its state becomes a state of decay. Its existence vanishes, and only consciousness remains — a consciousness that belongs to nothing and to no entity. At that station, the human being reaches the rank of ʿārif bi-Llāh (the one who knows through God), whereas otherwise he remains in the rank of those with knowledge *about* God. As Imam ʿAlī said, "Know God through God." The purpose of dhikr Allāh is not only that the human being should forget all but God, but that he should grasp that there is nothing other than God — as ʿAlī said: "When the heart is illumined by the remembrance of God, then the state of seeing after blindness and hearing after deafness arises." Wherever he turns, he beholds the Face of God:

> ⟪quran 2:115⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلِلَّهِ ٱلْمَشْرِقُ وَٱلْمَغْرِبُ ۚ فَأَيْنَمَا تُوَلُّوا۟ فَثَمَّ وَجْهُ ٱللَّهِ ۚ إِنَّ ٱللَّهَ وَسِعٌ عَلِيمٌۭ ‎⟫
> *And to Allah belong the east and the west, so wherever you turn yourselves or your faces there is the Face of Allah (and He is High above, over His Throne). Surely! Allah is All-Sufficient for His creatures' needs, All-Knowing.*
To God belong > the East and the West. Whichever way you turn, there is the Face of God. God is All-embracing, All-knowing.
> — **Quran 2:115 (inline widget)**

> ⟪quran 24:41⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَلَمْ تَرَ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يُسَبِّحُ لَهُۥ مَن فِى ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ وَٱلطَّيْرُ صَٓفَّتٍۢ ۖ كُلٌّۭ قَدْ عَلِمَ صَلَاتَهُۥ وَتَسْبِيحَهُۥ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ عَلِيمٌۢ بِمَا يَفْعَلُونَ ‎⟫
> *See you not (O Muhammad PBUH) that Allah, He it is Whom glorify whosoever is in the heavens and the earth, and the birds with wings out-spread (in their flight). Of each one He (Allah) knows indeed his Salat (prayer) and his glorification, [or everyone knows his Salat (prayer) and his glorification], and Allah is All-Aware of what they do.*
> do you not see that all those who are in the heavens and the earth, and the birds with outspread wings, glorify Allah? Each one knows its prayer and its glorification, and Allah knows what they do.
> — **Quran 24:41 (inline widget)**

In his sermons the Commander of the Faithful declares: "I am the sign of God Most High; I know the secrets of God; I am the limit of every limit; I am the companion of the rays of divine glory; I am the first, I am the last, I am the outward, I am the inward; I am the Face of God; I am the mirror of God, His pen, and His tablet; I am he who is called *al-ʿAlī* in the Gospels; I am he in whose hands are entrusted the secrets of the Messenger of God."

Though such words evoke the spectre of shirk to the outward-bound worshipper, those of inner vision grasp the true sense. If we first understand the line "I am the sign of God Most High," all that follows falls into place. In this sermon the Imam is identifying himself not with his own knowledge of God but with God's knowledge of Himself. The knowledge that God has of Himself is not the knowledge that the human being has of God. Knower, known, and knowledge become one when they reach the locus of this supreme knowledge — just as knowledge and existence become one when they reach the summit of consciousness. At this point the knower becomes self-aware, and knowledge is the consciousness that the Real has of itself. Consciousness gains access to this knowledge when it annihilates every particle of its existence in God. One must forget oneself in order to remember God; to become God's knowledge, everything must become what God Himself is. At that moment the human being himself becomes a ray of divine glory. All inward secrets ⟪ar:’السر‘⟫ — al-sirr — are concealed within the Ultimate Secret, which is the very Essence of God and which encompasses all the Names within itself. This is why he says, "I am the first, I am the last, I am the outward, I am the inward."

Then he calls himself the Face of God. This saying is understood in the light of his other comparison of himself to a mirror. When one looks into a mirror, the mirror itself ceases to exist; all the viewer's attention is on the face seen within. He does not even register the mirror's existence — that is, the mirror surrenders its identity. Conversely, when attention falls on the mirror, the face cannot be seen within it. So the mirror is identified with the form that the "I" beholds. At that moment the mirror and the form become one, though they remain two distinct objects. As long as one's attention is on the mirror, one wears a veil over the face; but the moment one turns to the face, the veil rises and unveils its servitude. Heedlessness of the mirror's existence is the analogue of the human being's annihilation, and attention to the image within is the analogue of the remembrance of God.

Here, too, we meet a paradox. The friends of God — those who know that they are nothing in the presence of God's divinity — are the very ones who rightly interpret God's Name and attributes. The attributes that belong to God are not theirs. This is why these saints and ṣafāʾ-bearers stand as representatives of God's most beautiful names. The light of these attributes shines through them because they have burnt the mirror of the heart with God's remembrance and wiped away every stain of egoism. Physicality cannot prevent them from reflecting this light. These are beings whose hearts dwell in paradise while their bodies labour in this world. Even while living in this world, their lives have been undone, as God has said:

> ⟪quran 29:64⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَمَا هَذِهِ ٱلْحَيَوٰةُ ٱلدُّنْيَآ إِلَّا لَهْوٌۭ وَلَعِبٌۭ ۚ وَإِنَّ ٱلدَّارَ ٱلْآخِرَةَ لَهِىَ ٱلْحَيَوَانُ ۚ لَوْ كَانُوا۟ يَعْلَمُونَ ‎⟫
> *And this life of the world is only amusement and play! Verily, the home of the Hereafter, that is the life indeed (i.e. the eternal life that will never end), if they but knew*
> And the life of this world is naught but a sport and a pastime; and the life of the Hereafter, if they but knew.
> — **Quran 29:64 (inline widget)**

Their instincts, then, are always tethered to the divine realities. When Imam ʿAlī was struck by the sword of Ibn Muljam, he instinctively cried: "By the Lord of the Kaʿba, I have triumphed!" At the moment of his death there was nothing but the remembrance of God; there was no god but God upon his tongue. He died upon the very wonder by which he had lived — upon the teaching of the Messenger of God, who said, "The most blessed death is to die in a state where your tongue is moist with the remembrance of God."

[^cite-59]: See Nahj al-Balāgha (compiled by al-Sharīf al-Raḍī), where Imam ʿAlī repeatedly identifies sincerity (ikhlāṣ) and the inward jihād against the self as the gateway to true worship, framing outward devotion without inward purification as essentially negligent.
[^cite-60]: Compare al-Sijistānī's discussion of the divine command "Be!" (kun) in Kitāb al-Yanābīʿ, where the imperative is treated as the originating word through which existents are summoned from non-being and remain suspended in the act of divine address.
[^cite-61]: See al-Sharīf al-Raḍī (ed.), Nahj al-Balāgha, Sermon 1, where Imam ʿAlī states that the perfection of sincerity (kamāl al-ikhlāṣ) lies in stripping God of attributes, since every attribute bears witness to its own otherness from the One it qualifies — the very passage paraphrased and unpacked in this chapter.

<!-- section 24 (id=5722, raw_sort=24): The Spirit Of The Intellect -->
## The Spirit of the Intellect

Our purpose is to outline some of the principal dimensions of the spiritual ethos of Mawlānā ʿAlī, with particular stress on its intellectual aspects, and to demonstrate the pivotal role played by the intellect (*al-ʿaql*) in the elaboration and assimilation of his teachings. One of our central aims is to show something of the *spirit of the intellect* in his worldview — a spirit that not only comprises the activities of the rational mind but surpasses them, encompassing domains not nowadays associated with the intellect at all, such as moral conduct and aesthetic sensibility. We further hope to demonstrate the manner in which this spiritual view of the intellect is directly related to the Qurʾānic concept of the spirit (*al-rūḥ*), and to explore some of the fruits of engaging deeply with this unifying spirit proper to the intellect.

It may be useful at the outset to clarify our preference for the word "intellect" rather than "reason" as the rendering of *ʿaql*. What we wish to evoke is the original meaning of the Latin *intellectus*: that faculty capable of a direct, contemplative vision of transcendent realities. Reason, by contrast, is of an indirect, discursive nature, bound to modes of discourse; it works with logic and arrives only at the mental concepts of those realities. Through the intellect one is able to contemplate, or "see," the Absolute; through reason alone one can only think about it.

Three factors inhere in the spirit of the intellectual perspective considered here: the centrality of revelation, the harmony between revelation and the intellect, and the distinction between the intellect (conceived as the principle of the articulation of consciousness) and reason.

Beginning with the first, one must assert that everything in Mawlānā ʿAlī's life and teaching is centred upon the Qurʾānic revelation and its embodiment in the Prophet's conduct. Both the thought and the life of Mawlānā ʿAlī can be read as a kind of spiritual commentary — a *taʾwīl* (esoteric exegesis) — upon the sacred text. On the doctrinal plane, it can be asserted with confidence that he was one possessing an innate aptitude for the task of *taʾwīl*. On the existential plane too, his life unfolds as a dramatic disclosure of the inner meaning of the revealed text, from his birth within the most holy precinct of the Kaʿba to his martyrdom in the mosque of Kūfa. His refusal ever to stop short at the surface of things — whether religious, ethical, or political — manifested an unflinching fidelity to the sacred substance of the religion, a fidelity that entailed both intellectual penetration inwardly and combative resistance outwardly. The saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is of considerable importance in this connection: *ʿAlī will fight for the taʾwīl of the Qurʾān as I fought for its tanzīl*.[^cite-62]

As mentioned earlier, Mawlānā ʿAlī's short caliphate was dominated by bloody civil wars. It can well be argued that all three of these wars were fought precisely for the sake of the correct interpretation and application of the Qurʾān — for the clarification of its fundamental message (*tawḥīd*, divine oneness) and for the realisation of that oneness on every spiritual and social level. This claim is substantiated in graphic manner by his response to a bedouin who, just as the battle of the Camel (*al-Jamal*) was about to begin, came to him and asked: "O Commander of the Faithful! Do you say that Allah is one?" Those present remonstrated with the man, telling him that this was neither the time nor the place for such questions. Mawlānā ʿAlī, however, intervened: "Leave him, for surely what the bedouin wishes is what we wish for the people."

We may begin our reflections on the spirituality of Mawlānā ʿAlī by attending to this exchange. The response touches upon the principle that right intention takes priority over outward action, whatever that action may be. For Mawlānā ʿAlī, the most fundamental intention motivating every act — religious in the ordinary sense or military as in the present context — was knowledge of the true nature of ultimate reality. What he "wishes for the people," then, is precisely this knowledge, which must lie at the very heart of all concerns, motivations and actions, on the battlefield no less than upon the prayer rug. There is therefore no time and no place where the question — *what do you mean by saying Allah is one?* — is out of place. It was for the sake of the correct knowledge of the divine that Mawlānā ʿAlī was fighting in the first place, in fulfilment of the Prophet's prophecy.

On the very field of battle, then, Mawlānā ʿAlī delivers a discourse on the meaning of divine oneness. The very first portion of his reply contains a key to his whole spiritual ethos. He refutes the notion that Allah's oneness could in any way be a "numerical" or "countable" oneness, telling the bedouin that it is not permissible to say "one" while having in mind any numerical conception, for *that which has no second does not enter into the category of numbers*.

"That which has no second" is not merely a divinity to which no partner can be ascribed. It is that reality which has no "otherness" at all — the Real, *al-Ḥaqq*, at once transcendent and immanent, beyond all things yet manifested by all things, inaccessible and inalienable together. In these few words Mawlānā ʿAlī effects, for those who have ears to hear, a shift of consciousness: from the dogmatic notion of one God versus many gods to the mystical apprehension of the one and only Reality, in the face of which everything else is but appearance. All things become perceived as so many outward phenomena which call out to be deciphered — as signs (*āyāt*) pointing to that of which they are manifestations. To retrace these appearances back to their source and origin is the literal sense of *taʾwīl*: returning to the *awwal*, the First.[^cite-63] On one hand, the Real is described as the beginning and end of all things, the apparent and the hidden — there is nothing in being but this one Reality:

> ⟪quran 57:3⟫
> ⟪ar:‏هُوَ ٱلْأَوَّلُ وَٱلْآخِرُ وَٱلظَّهِرُ وَٱلْبَاطِنُ ۖ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَىْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ‎⟫
> *He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward. And He is the All-Knower of every thing.*

On the other, apparent multiplicity must be brought back to real oneness, as Allah says:

> ⟪quran 41:53⟫
> ⟪ar:‏سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَتِنَا فِى ٱلأٓفَاقِ وَفِىٓ أَنفُسِهِمْ حَتَّىٰ يَتَبَيَّنَ لَهُمْ أَنَّهُ ٱلْحَقُّ ۗ أَوَلَمْ يَكْفِ بِرَبِّكَ أَنَّهُۥ عَلَىٰ كُلِّ شَىْءٍۢ شَهِيدٌ ‎⟫
> *We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their own selves, until it becomes manifest to them that this is the truth.*

In one saying attributed to Mawlānā ʿAlī, there is a reference to the Prophet as the first whom Allah instructed in the science of *taʾwīl*; the Prophet in turn taught Mawlānā ʿAlī. According to the Prophet himself, *truly, ʿAlī is with the Qurʾān, and the Qurʾān is with ʿAlī*. In another famous saying, Mawlānā ʿAlī claimed to be able to load seventy camels with the pages of the commentary on Sūrat al-Fātiḥa; and his cousin Ibn ʿAbbās professed to have learnt the science of interpretation from him.

In this connection the following saying is of fundamental importance: *The Qurʾān is a book inscribed between two covers; it does not speak with a tongue, and it cannot do without an interpreter* (⟪ar:لا بُدَّا مِنَ التَّرجُمٰن⟫). The exchange between revelation and the intellect is further underlined in the saying *The prophet of a man is the interpreter of his intellect* (⟪ar:رَاسُ الرَّجَل تَرجُمان عَقلِہ⟫). Elsewhere we find an explicit correspondence between the intellect and the inner messenger: the intellect, in his words, is *the messenger of the Real*.[^cite-64]

Thus the teachings of Mawlānā ʿAlī, in which his spiritual ethos finds expression, should be read essentially as creative interpretations of the Qurʾānic revelation. They initiate a movement from the letters of the revelation to its spirit, and from the spirit of the revelation to the spirit of the Revealer:

Letters of revelation → Spirit of Revelation → Revealer (*al-Ḥaqq*)

This can be conceived as a process of retracing — going back to the source, the origin, the *awwal* — accomplished in the measure that the spirit of the intellect is enlivened within the interpreter. Yet the Revealer remains, of necessity, concealed as the essence of the divine Reality even as He is revealed through revelation. The divine Reality in itself is too great to be exhausted in words; only the forms of its manifestation are susceptible of revelation. This dialectical interplay between form and the formless, the evident and the hidden, the revealed and the concealed, lies at the root of the paradoxical mode of expression for which Mawlānā ʿAlī is renowned.

One of the most striking aspects of his perspective is the way it reveals the hollowness of many of the falsely or excessively polarised dichotomies which later came to characterise — or be imposed upon — Islamic thought. This dichotomy between intellect and revelation appears in various polarised ramifications: reason versus faith; convention (*sunna*) versus nature (*ṭabīʿa*); imitation (*taqlīd*) versus verification (*taḥqīq*); piety (*taqwā*) set against inquiry; and so on. In Mawlānā ʿAlī's ethos, however, intellect and revelation are not mutually exclusive but complementary — and, at the highest level, indeed inseparable, since truth is one. While the human intellect cannot in any way be equated with divine revelation, the spirit animating the intellect is not restricted by human limitations. It is this spirit which perceives and grasps the divine quality of revelation.

The divine spirit articulating the form and infusing the content of revelation must be understandable to some degree by human consciousness. Were it otherwise, revelation would be no more than a set of rules and regulations imposed on the will, rather than a source of creative inspiration for the intellect. It cannot be too strongly stressed that divine revelation presupposes a creative engagement with all the resources of the intellect; it does not curtail the operations of reason and impose some unintelligible decree. This is precisely what the Qurʾān itself repeatedly calls for.

The revealed text is silent, and "speaks" only through the interpreter, the intellect. This view of the relationship between intellect and revelation rigorously excludes every form of literalism and superficiality. It excludes any attempt to confine meaning within a flat, unilateral reading. On the contrary, it seeks the opposite. Rather than enclose meaning, the tendency generated here serves to *disclose* it — to open up the inner horizons of multiple levels of meaning hidden within the text. Such multidimensional significance is plumbed in accordance with the depth and penetration of the interpreter's intellect. This prophetic intellect rejoins the very source of revelation; for it is this inner prophet, this inmost degree of consciousness, that ultimately enables one to confirm as true the verses of the outer revelation. It validates them experientially, not merely affirms them dogmatically. It is for this reason that Mawlānā ʿAlī says, in the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha*, that one of the purposes of revelation — of the sending forth of prophets by Allah to humankind — was *to unearth for them the buried treasures of the intellects* (⟪ar:دفائن العقول⟫).[^cite-65]

This saying constitutes one of the keys of the spiritual ethos under consideration: the intellect is not stifled by revelation; rather, it is enlivened by it. Revelation is a loud and clear summons to the spiritual depths of the intellect; it cultivates and brings to fruition the seeds of consciousness planted there by Allah. The spirit of one's intellect cannot, then, be alien to that which descends as revelation from Allah; on the contrary, it must be fundamentally akin to it. It is precisely for this reason that one can have absolute certainty that revelation indeed comes from the Absolute. The matter is not so much one of setting up a dichotomy between intellect and revelation, but of seeing the intellect *as* revelation — or at least as one mode of revelation. The intellect is the God-given means by which the individual comes to discern, affirm and plumb the divine revelations outside himself: both those of the signs (*āyāt*) of scripture and those constituted by the signs (*āyāt*) of the natural world. If the human intellect can perceive and recognise revelation, it is because both are rooted in the same ultimate source. Prophetic revelation awakens and brings to life the depths of the human intellect; it does not replace or displace it. From this perspective, the revealed text is not some extraneous source set over against the intelligence, but rather the objective, outward expression of the very principles which the intellect itself yields to the inner prophet. By "intellect," then, we mean not simply the faculty of reasoning, but the very source of consciousness articulating the human spirit.[^cite-66]

The intellect contains unsuspected depths — buried treasures — and is thus far from being exhausted by its surface operations, or by the cognitions and reasonings proper to its purely mental dimension. These considerations bring us once again to the relationship between intellect and reason. Just as there can be no contradiction whatsoever between **revelation** and **intellect**, one must likewise avoid setting up a false dichotomy between **reason** and **intellect**. Reason is to be perceived as one of the modes of the intellect. Reason no more contradicts intellect than intellect contradicts revelation. Reason is a mode of the intellect, but it is not to be coarsely equated with it. Equally, one cannot reduce the universal objectivity of revelation to the subjective specificities of any one individual's intellect, or to his or her "private" revelation. To equate reason with intellect would be tantamount to denying the existence of the buried treasures of the intellect — those dimensions which transcend rationality and lie, at first, beneath and hidden from the rational surface of the intellect. The revelation constituted by the intellect is embedded within the very creation of the human being; and it is this intellect which most critically defines the human being as such.

Rather than setting intellect against revelation, each is to be seen in the light of the other. The substance of revelation cannot be engaged except through the intellect; and the buried treasures of the intellect cannot be disclosed except through revelation. This understanding of the intellect brings us close to what Mawlānā ʿAlī refers to as the *heart*: that innermost mode of perception capable of "seeing" Allah. It is the heart that can see Allah — not through eyesight, but through the spiritual insight generated by the *true principles of faith* (*ḥaqāʾiq al-īmān*). As he puts it, in response to a questioner who asked whether he had seen his Lord: *Eyes see Him not through sight's observation; but hearts see Him through the true principles and beliefs of faith.*

To return to the relationship between intellect and reason, one may say that between the spiritual depths of the intellect and its own rational surface there is both continuity and discontinuity. The continuity is manifest in the evident fact that all thinking begins with concepts conveying intelligible meaning. Thought is initially concerned with ideas rationally discernible, and these must bear some relation to the realities they express. But there is also discontinuity between the spiritual and the rational aspects of the intellect, in that reason halts at the ideas it has *de-fined* — the Arabic word for definition, *ḥadd*, expresses at once a conceptual definition and a limitation or boundary. To go beyond the limits imposed by the rational act of definition, something more than reason is necessary. This "something more" is what makes the ethos we are considering a spiritual one rather than a rationalistic one. It is "rational" in the sense that its form is open to the scrutiny of reason; but it is not "rationalistic," inasmuch as its essence is not predicated on reason alone.

Mawlānā ʿAlī frequently hints, in highly allusive language, at the mysteries and the marvels — the unearthed treasures of the intellect — that he has directly witnessed by the eye of his purified heart. He also alludes indirectly to this supra-rational mode of awareness through the bewildering use of apophatic (knowledge of God obtained through negation) discourse and paradoxical utterance, which fills the works attributed to him. It seems that, in this ethos, the higher the truth the deeper the paradox. The incommensurability between the reality in itself and the human conceptions formed of it is effectively evoked by this creative use of paradox. Each human conception of reality must first be deconstructed in order to be reconstructed. The paradoxes of Mawlānā ʿAlī feed directly into modes of spiritual intuition, contemplation and realisation of that which reason, of itself, is incapable of attaining. It is this intuition and contemplation which *re-members*, or reconstructs, the spiritual realities that transcend the categories of reason. The value of paradox, antinomy (a contradiction between two beliefs or conclusions that are each in themselves reasonable), apophasis and negation rests entirely upon the various positive realities the intellect can plumb, becoming aware thereby of its own hidden depths. The resulting consciousness does not so much negate reason as situate it, revealing the proper limits of its sphere of operation. It shows that reason alone cannot comprehend the higher realities, nor express them fully, however indispensable it may be in initiating the cognitive movement toward them.[^cite-67]

Thus, when Mawlānā ʿAlī negates the possibility of human conceptions encompassing the divine Reality, what is negated is the limitation that is the inescapable corollary (a proposition following from one already proved) of all such conceptions — not the positive content, the *maʿnā* or spiritual meaning of the conception itself. The conception remains a necessary starting point for a process of assimilation that engages a deeper level of awareness than that defined by the mind, and which therefore extends far beyond the domain of speculative thought. In this light, the negation of the limitations of human conception should be seen as the prelude to an absolute affirmation: the affirmation of the Absolute, which has no limitation. The spiritual negation of conceptual limitation is of an upward, constructive order, not of a nihilistic (rejecting all religious and moral principles in the belief that life is meaningless), deconstructive one. Nowhere is this style of discourse more apparent than in the famous opening lines of the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha*:

> Praise be to God, whose praise those who speak cannot deliver, whose graces those who count cannot number, whose rightful due those who strive cannot render; He who cannot be grasped by aspirations, nor fathomed by profound intuitions; He whose attribute has no binding limitation, no existing description, no time appointed, no term extended.

The divine attributes (*ṣifa*) cannot be confined to a definition (*laysa li-ṣifatihi ḥaddun maḥdūd*), and yet they are affirmed as real. The divine is described (*mawṣūf*) by the very words of the Qurʾānic revelation, even though He has, properly speaking, no "description." For example, Allah is described as having the attributes the Merciful, the Compassionate, the Almighty, and so on. Yet Mawlānā ʿAlī affirms that these very attributes cannot be defined, bounded, or described. The paradox is sharpened still further when he speaks of the perfection of *ikhlāṣ* — the rendering sincere, the purification of one's devotion to Allah and of one's conception of Allah. This is to divest (*nafy*) Allah of all attributes. On one hand the attributes are affirmed; on the other they are to be negated. The rational mind can think of Allah only in terms of the attributes verbally expressed in the Qurʾān; yet here Mawlānā ʿAlī appears to negate those same attributes, depriving the mind of any convenient conceptual handle by which to grasp the divine nature. This simultaneous affirmation and negation raises an apparently irresolvable contradiction. In doing so, however, it enhances receptivity to a deeper mode of knowing. The discourse is designed to awaken a latent awareness inscribed in the heart, in the depths of the intellect. That awareness then invites the seeker to "dive" into the depths of the knowledge buried within the intellect. The very shock of the apparent contradiction precipitates a leap of consciousness from the formal activity of reason to the supra-rational contemplation of the spirit.

Consider the words spoken by Mawlānā ʿAlī immediately after affirming that his heart had indeed witnessed Allah, not through outward vision but through the realities of faith: *My Lord is not described in terms of dimension* (⟪ar:بُعْد⟫), *nor motion* (⟪ar:حرکۃ⟫), *nor rest* (⟪ar:سکون⟫), *nor through coming or going. He is subtle in subtlety, but not described by subtlety. He is magnificent in magnificence, but not described by magnificence. He is great in greatness, but not described by greatness.*

Once more the attributes are at once affirmed and denied. Allah possesses these attributes, but is not reducible to them. We can begin to think of Allah through these categories, but we cannot reduce Him to our limited conceptions. The attributes afford the mind something by which the divine nature can be understood, yet this understanding remains fragmentary, never exhaustive. It must therefore be negated, so that a fuller understanding may be gained. The initial conceptual orientation to the divine Reality needs both negation and sublimation: negation as a mental conception, while the positive content of the conception is elevated, by spiritual intuition, to a higher rank. Only then can the two-dimensional portrait defined by reason be given the third dimension of depth. But this third dimension emerges only once the insufficiency of the rational depiction has been clearly seen. Paradox produces the cognitive shock which exposes the pretension of reason in its effort to disclose the nature of the Absolute.

One sees in the discourses of Mawlānā ʿAlī a repeated combination of affirmation and denial that mirrors the negation and affirmation of the first testimony of Islam: *lā ilāha*, "no god," as the negation (*nafy*); and *illā Allāh*, "except Allah," as the affirmation (*ithbāt*). This helps us see that the perfection of *ikhlāṣ*, as described by Mawlānā ʿAlī, is gained by the negation of the attributes *in order to* affirm the infinitude of the divine Reality. Each attribute is thus to be negated, but only insofar as it is conceived by the limited mind. The negation is necessary because, at the mental level, each attribute excludes not only the others but also the very object of attribution itself — the divine Essence. For any concept to take shape in the mind, defined ideas are required; and where there are definitions, there are limits, and so exclusions. The reality of the attribute, however, is unlimited by virtue of its absolute identity with the Essence. The attribute that has been "negated" is paradoxically affirmed as being nothing but Allah: *illā Allāh*. One can therefore place each negated attribute in the position of the *nafy* of the first testimony, arriving at such formulations as *lā raḥmāna illā ʾllāh* — "There is no All-Compassionate but Allah" — and *lā raḥīma illā ʾllāh* — "There is no All-Merciful but Allah."

In this respect, the apparent negation of the attributes not only becomes an affirmation of their true identity; it also disconnects them from any restrictive association with the poverty of mental conception. The attributes are not superimposed upon the Essence; they are absolutely one with it. They are differentiable from the Essence only in conception, not in existence. The Essence does indeed comprise all the mentioned attributes — greatness, subtlety, and the rest — and is also infinitely more than the sum of them. The attributes are infinite only within, and as, the Essence. Mawlānā ʿAlī's explanation of this paradox can be seen, in effect, as a commentary upon the Qurʾānic verse:

> ⟪quran 23:91⟫
> ⟪ar:‏مَا ٱتَّخَذَ ٱللَّهُ مِن وَلَدٍۢ وَمَا كَانَ مَعَهُۥ مِنْ إِلَهٍ ۚ إِذًۭا لَّذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَهٍۭ بِمَا خَلَقَ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَىٰ بَعْضٍۢ ۚ سُبْحَنَ ٱللَّهِ عَمَّا يَصِفُونَ ‎⟫
> *Glorified be Allah above all that they attribute to Him!*

The infinitude of Allah demands the negation of all that the mind can conceive through reason on the human plane. It does not, however, negate what the heart can "see" through spiritual vision. This very negation at the level of reason calls out to something deeper; and that deeper level can in turn only be understood in the light of spiritual practice, always assumed as the reflective, devotional and contemplative context for the hearers of Mawlānā ʿAlī's sermons. The heart, as the symbolic seat of contemplative intelligence, calls out to be polished, and this is precisely what the remembrance of Allah accomplishes. It is for this reason that the remembrance of Allah (*dhikr Allāh*), according to Mawlānā ʿAlī, is the most excellent form of worship.

Even at the level of practice there is a further paradox: the reality of remembrance is not attained until *you forget yourself* in your remembrance of Allah. It is not only the rational mind and its concepts that must be negated or deconstructed; it is the individual soul itself — as the locus of all cognition, rational or supra-rational — that must finally be negated. This final denouement, annihilation (*fanāʾ*), is the ultimate form of paradox.

In the perspective of Mawlānā ʿAlī, the intellect must be seen, first and foremost, as a spiritual faculty; and the spirit must be appreciated as not only the source of the intellect but also the source of life itself. Without the intellect the spirit is reduced to an unconscious existence; without the spirit the intellect tends toward lifeless thought. This is why the Qurʾān, in describing the creation of the human being, states that Allah breathed His spirit into Adam (peace be upon him):

> ⟪quran 32:7⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِىٓ أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَىْءٍ خَلَقَهُۥ ۖ وَبَدَأَ خَلْقَ ٱلْإِنسَنِ مِن طِينٍۢ ‎⟫
> *Who made everything He has created good, and He began the creation of man from clay.*

> ⟪quran 32:8⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ثُمَّ جَعَلَ نَسْلَهُۥ مِن سُلَلَةٍۢ مِّن مَّآءٍۢ مَّهِينٍۢ ‎⟫
> *Then He made his offspring from semen of worthless water.*

> ⟪quran 32:9⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ثُمَّ سَوَّىٰهُ وَنَفَخَ فِيهِ مِن رُّوحِهِۦ ۖ وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ ٱلسَّمْعَ وَٱلْأَبْصَرَ وَٱلْأَفْـِٔدَةَ ۚ قَلِيلًۭا مَّا تَشْكُرُونَ ‎⟫
> *Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and breathed into him the soul, and He gave you hearing, sight and hearts. Little is the thanks you give!*

The following lines from the first sermon are among the many passages in which this infusion of the divine spirit into the human being is commented upon by Mawlānā ʿAlī:

> Then Allah (may He be glorified), gathered up a quantity of dust, from some hard earth and some soft, some sweet earth and some salty, and poured water onto it until it was pure, then kneaded into it some moisture until it stuck together. He then fashioned from this, a form with curves and joints, limbs and parts. He compacted it until it held tightly together. He solidified it until it became as sounding clay, assigning to it a measured duration and a known consummation. Then He breathed into it of His spirit, and it stood up as a human being, endowed with intellectual powers with which to reflect, thoughts by which to conduct himself, limbs to put to service, instruments at his disposal, and knowledge with which to discern between the true and the false.

In other words, it is the *in-spiration* — literally the in-breathing, the insertion of the spirit — of Allah's breath that produces consciousness and life. Death, by contrast, is *ex-piration*: the departure of the spirit. To live in the absence of any intention to be true to one's spirit is itself a kind of death. Through this spirit, inanimate, unconscious matter is brought to life and given awareness of itself; it generates knowledge not only of other forms but also of the self. This intimate intertwining of the gift of life with the capacity to know — existence that is conscious, knowledge that is existential — is one of the foundations of any genuinely spiritual perspective. It is not a matter of contrasting the spirit with the body, but of realising that the infusion of the spirit *into* the body is what brings the body to life. For this reason, "spiritual" knowledge is precisely what *enlivens hearts*:

> ⟪quran 8:24⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ ٱسْتَجِيبُوا۟ لِلَّهِ وَلِلرَّسُولِ إِذَا دَعَاكُمْ لِمَا يُحْيِيكُمْ ۖ وَٱعْلَمُوٓا۟ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يَحُولُ بَيْنَ ٱلْمَرْءِ وَقَلْبِهِۦ وَأَنَّهُۥٓ إِلَيْهِ تُحْشَرُونَ ‎⟫
> *O you who believe! Answer Allah and His messenger when he calls you to that which will give you life, and know that Allah comes in between a person and his heart. And verily to Him you shall be gathered.*

According to Mawlānā ʿAlī, one of the most important aspects of this "call" from Allah to human beings is precisely to remind them of the buried treasures of the intellect.

Existence is the bestowal of the conscious spirit upon the body. Hence the *ʿaql* — the intellect, which is the principle of articulation for the spirit — is not merely a mental faculty; it is the constitution of our true life. The mental faculty is one mode of the intellect's operation, but by no means the only one. According to the sayings of Mawlānā ʿAlī, *ʿaql* is multi-faceted, comprising aspects which are rarely associated, in our times, with intelligence.

To be "spiritual," then, means to be true to one's intellect — to the treasures buried deep within it, and not merely to the rational functions operating upon its surface. For Mawlānā ʿAlī, the true intellectual (*al-ʿāqil*) is one who not only thinks correctly but also acts ethically, and who, at the deepest level, seeks to realise the ultimate Reality. He defines the intellectual as *one who puts all things in their proper place*. Since this is also the very definition of justice, only the true intellectual can be fully just; only one with the capacity to see things as they truly are is able to put them in their right place. Thought, action and realisation are therefore all the concern of the true intellectual, the *ʿāqil*. Correct thinking, impeccable virtue and authentic being are inseparably bound up with the intellect in the spiritual ethos we are considering. These aspects of the ethos will become clearer in the light of Mawlānā's discourse to Kumayl, to which we now turn.

[^cite-62]: This famous prophetic dictum on ʿAlī and *taʾwīl* is repeatedly invoked by Fatimid-era authors as the cornerstone of the imamic interpretive office. See al-Qāḍī al-Nuʿmān, *Asās al-Taʾwīl*, where the inheritance of the *taʾwīl* function from the Prophet to ʿAlī, and from him to the line of imams, is set out as the foundational premise of the work.

[^cite-63]: On *taʾwīl* as the act of returning a thing to its *awwal* (origin), see al-Qāḍī al-Nuʿmān, *Asās al-Taʾwīl*, the introductory chapters on the meaning and necessity of esoteric exegesis. The same etymological move underlies the structure of Jaʿfar ibn Manṣūr al-Yaman's *al-Kashf*.

[^cite-64]: The correspondence between the inner intellect and the outer Messenger is developed at length by Ḥamīd al-Dīn al-Kirmānī in *Rāḥat al-ʿAql*, where the First Intellect (*al-ʿAql al-Awwal*) is identified as the ontological prototype of prophetic revelation, and the partial intellect in the human being as its receiving correlate.

[^cite-65]: ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib, *Nahj al-Balāgha*, sermon 1 (compiled by al-Sharīf al-Raḍī).
